Dr. M. PEHEY,
M.R.U.S., L.S A , &c.,
SPECIALIST for tlie Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical Bowl of N.S. W. Lute House Sur-
geon to the Great Northern Hospital. London
I am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
Of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the tieatm-nt.of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified 91 on in the treatment of
.Clirpnlc and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
gersons- The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
eld in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph, No\ v .
,6th 7th and Bth, 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified .lion entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from these disease*, who are scattered over all parts qf
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertised.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not only
Strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists, but T also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
Wr Qnallfleallons I Invite the fullest investieatlon Into. For some considerable time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities ot observin ; the many phases of the Nervous and
Sexua System, and to be oine acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure. ...
' Mv sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me lie is informed at the
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
If I undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete autl Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of those diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.

WORD COMPETITION.
STOCKTON SCHOOL OF ARTS.
The Committee of the above Institution offers a prize
of t'PI 10s. to the competitor Who can make the most
A ords, under” conditions hereunder, from the word
“ Mu’" lei pal Hies.” As a second prize, for the second
highest number of tvords, a tropby will be given. No
word will be accepted that cannot be found in either the
Imperial or Champers Etymological Dictionary. Provided,
however, that all plurals of such words may be given as
separate words. No word must be used more than once.
T ic fact that any word might be made more than once by
usincr duplicate letters contained in the competitive word
s ialf not affect this last point. No word will be allowed
i hat contains any letter oftener than the same occurs in
the competitive word. Competitors should arrange their
words alphabetically. No member of the Committee will
he allowed to compete. All lists must be sent in, addressed
lo the undersigned, on or before 25th March next. Every
list must be accompanied by a fee of 2s. 6d. Any com-
pel itor wishing to have his list returned, must send
stamped addressed envelope for the purpose. The result
will be published in the issue of this paper of 4th April
11. A. GRAHAM, Secretary.

European destinations, and have at San Francisco choice
of the unrivalled Atlantic Steamship Lines of the Cunard,
Inman, White Star, and other Companies.

for San Francisco, and 2501 b. “ overland ” free of charge,

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 14, 1891.
Australian Federation.
The time is almost at hand for an effort to
be made towards the solution of the most
momentous problem which, so far, has pre-
sented itself to the Australian people. It is
a problem also which, should no solution or,
worse still, a false one, be supplied, may
mean much heartburning in the former case
and possible disintegration in the latter.
The question is that known as Federation,
and has been before the public in various
rather shadowy forms during the last thirty
years and over. For many reasons —some
rather obscure, while others are attractively
Obvious —a certain prominence has of late
been given to the subject by the action of
Sir Henry Pahkes. At this time of day it
is almost supererogatory to point out that the
N.S.W. Premier tills no humble niche in
that great Pantheon of prestidigitators whose
public lives are guided by two useful rules—
to stick to office like a reef to a Pacific atoll,
and to stamp as their own the ideas of others.
In a political sense these qualities are of the
first order of merit and, in justice to the
Premier, it must be conceded that he
possesses them in excelsis. It can be confi-
dently stated that, if the late Foreign-
trade majority at the N.S.W. general elec-
tion had consisted of thirty instead of three,
or if the principle of Protection to native
industries were not a power in the land, the
question of Federation would still remain in
solution and show no signs of crystallising.
# * *
It may be said that, as intercolonial Free-
trade with Protection against the outside
world would be the obvious result of Federa-
tion, Sir Henry Parses would be merely
signing his own administrative death-warrant
by pressing for the creation of a Congress in
which he and his fiscal followers would be in
a hopeless minority. This seems plausible
enough. But those who adopt such a line
of reasoning are not properly acquainted
with the political dodges that attend on
representative government like a shadow
For instance, the Federation cry, combined
with a proposal to let the fiscal question
alone till the larger one of union was settled,
would be by no means a bad one with which
to face a general election. Again, these
negotiations will necessarily take time, and
time is to Premier Parkes at present as
precious as it was to Sister Anne in the
story, when M. Bluebeard was heard coming
up the stairs for her head Even at the
worst, Sir Henry Parkes in a fiscal minority
in a Federal Congress would have far greater
chances than when in a similar minority in
a local Parliament. Indeed, it is far from
improbable that he would be found at the
head of « fJovevnment which held office
while an economic measure it opposed was
passed, just as in Sydney he remained in
office while the measure for paying members,
towards which he expressed uncompromising
hostility, became law. His action on the
occasion of the rejection of that measure by
the N.S.W. Upper House was probably the
finest piece of what is known in histrionism
as “gag” that was ever witnessed by those
privileged to see it. It showed that if
“ Mister Gladstone ” was his mentor,
Disraeli was his model.
# # *
Federation is of two kinds—good and bad.
Any closer welding of the various Australian
colonies that does not point to a future of
Independence and Republicanism is bad from
the start. The native-born Australians, and
those who have made the world’s largest
island their home, might be classed among
the meanest of men if they saw for that home
nothing beyond a career of perpetual pro-
vincialism. It would be just as sensible to
expect the earth to revolve round the moon,
or Jupiter round one of his satellites, as to
expect a huge island to revolve in political
perpetuity round an islet. It is merely look-
ing for the tail to wag the dog. There is
a name, and by no means a flattering one,
for the man who in private life will not
strike out for himself but prefers to hang to
others’ coat-tails. But no term is too oppro-
brious for the nation which, with a geogra-
phical dot second to none the world over,
calmly makes up its mind to perpetual
subordination. Yet this is the precise
meaning of “Federation under the Crown.”
It means tinsel titles for the vulgar and the
vain ; irritating surveillance by distant aud
foreign officials who never saw nor wished to
sec this great southern land, and, in thp end,
a violent wrench, not unaccompanied per-
haps by bloodshed, when the generation that
came up out of Egypt has passed away to be
succeeded by a more independent and virile
one.
* * *
The primary factor then of a vicious
federation is that of an ignoble future. But
it is not the only one. A union might take
place of a highly objectionable and even
dangerous character, even if independence
were promised either now or at any time
that Australia demanded it. At the very
threshold it may be laid down as axiomatic
that any union which leaves a great question
to be settled by future generations, as the
slavery question was left by the Americans
of a hundred years ago, is perilous. And
it would be a fecund source of future bicker-
ings, or worse, to let five States only instead
of fifty constitute the units of the Australian
Confederation. The Americans were forced
to legalise slavery a century ago, or else split
into warring Republics with the chance of
being re-conquered in detail before the ink
on their charter of freedom was dry. The
world knows how the question of negro
bondage was ultimately settled a generation
since. The question of Australian territory
would in all probability be settled in a
similarly drastic fashion if the mistake of
permitting a continent to be unequally
divided into a small number of States
instead of equally into a large number, was
at the outset. In this readjustment there
need be no difficulty, for Territories could
be erected into States as in America when
an adequate population entitled them to the
promotion.
The chief elements of a sound federation
are (a) that ultimate Independence and Re-
publicanism be the recognised goal ; (6),
that a large number of States and Territories
be carved out of the present provinces, so
that no colony or group of colonies will be
likely to attain undue weight; (c), that the
post of Governor-General be elective, or at
least that the united provinces have an
absolute veto on the British appointment;
(d), that titles and - privileges of all kinds,
except those derived from office, be at once
abolished. There is no reason why a con-
federacy whose foundations were thus laid deep
and strong should not for all time preserve
at least one of the world’s great divisions
from that curse of war which has filled all
other continents with want, and blood, and
tears. But there is every reason to fear that
a league of a less pronounced character, such
a league as in all likelihood will be proposed
at the coming conference, will begin by
tying a knot which a subsequent generation
will have to cut with the sword. More than
ten years elapsed from the declaration of
American independence to the promulgation
of the Constitution, That Constitution has
lasted almost unchanged to the present, and
no shot would ever have been necessary for
its preservation but for the abysmal wrong
of slavery which, introduced by the British,
hung over the land like a pall till, in our own
day, a million men laid down their lives to
lift it. The great men who drafted the
American co-partnership saw r clearly what
the ultimate result of slavery would be, but
they were powerless to avert it. No such
disintegrating question, however, presents
itself to the Australian delegates. They
must create one for themselves, and, ap-
parently, may be trusted to raise a goblin
which by and bye will have to be laid by
force on the Murray or on the Darling.
» # *
We have said that, apart from his penchant
for affixing his own trade-mark to the mental
manufactures of others, Premier Pakkes
foremost characteristic is, like the Vicar of
Bray, to hold office at all hazards. It is
because a careful study of his career has
shown us how all subjects, political and
private, are viewed by him from a purely
personal standpoint—which might be formu-
lated in the words “how can I best bend
them to my profit I”—that wo decline to trust
him in this great question of laying the
foundation-stone of a peaceful and progres-
sive Australian Democracy. It remains to
examine two other facets of the subject
which are of the first importance. They are
(a) what the certain result wquld be to all
Australia outside Victoria, of a federation
of the five continental colonies; and (h) what
the chances of foreign invasion would be in
the event of a Declaration of Independence
either at present or in the near future.
■* * *
It is clear that Federation would be fol-
lowed without delay by continental Freetrade
combined with a stiff tariff on imports.
There are men who affect to deny this, just
as there are those who cannot make up their
minds concerning the earth’s rotundity, but
it is impossible to refrain from doubting the
sincerity of both. With the fiscal parties
balanced in New South Wales, while among
the other partners to the league the pre-
ponderance of Protectionists was overwhelm-
ing, the result admits of no argument.
Owing to the long fostering regime, the
manufactures of Victoria have reached a
point so far ahead of those of the other
colonies that, except in special cases, competi-
tion would for long be hopeless. Whether in
war or peace, when once a nation or a province
gets a long start of another, it is next to im-
possible to catch up. Three months in 1870
sufficed to bring France to her knees. Could
she but have obtained time to stand erect
and pull herself together before the final
fiasco at Paris, things might have been
different. But the Germans knew better
than to allow her. With Victorian capital
spread prodigally and thickly over Biverina,
Queensland, and other places, and with A ic-
toria manufacturing goods of a high quality
and a low price, what, in a few years, would
become of the boasted supremacy, or even
equality, of New South W ales ? It is idle to
say that manufactures will follow coal and
iron. They have not done so in Australia,
owing to the supineness of one portion of
the people, the selfishness of a second, and
the scheming of a third. Federate then
“ under the Crown,” with the provinces at
their present size, followed by Freetrade
from Carpentaria to and for years
and years you throw the whole commercial
supremacy into the arms of Melbourne —in
a word, cut the throat of New South Wales
as dexterously as Castlekeagh cut Ireland’s.
* * *
Let us now consider the question of in-
vasion in the event of independence. In a
late London magazine a military officer of
high rank has expressed the opinion that the
world might be searched in vain for a people
at once so rich and so helpless as the Aus-
tralians. He considers that, in the event of
the cessation of British sovereignty, no time
would be lost before the predatory powers of
Europe raced with each other for the posses-
sion of the whole or a part of such a wind-
fall. In commending this view the Sydney
Morning Herald stated that in the event of
England’s withdrawal any of the great
Powers could send here a sufficient force to
entrench itself and defy all Australia to shift
it. This is not only true, but its truth is too
obvious to be disputed. Any nation with
command of the sea could seize any un-
occupied and advantageously-situated spot
on the coast of any country, and, if allowed
time, fortify it in such a manner as to be
next to impregnable. The whole power of
Spain and France combined would probably
fail now, as it failed before, in taking Gib-
raltar—so long as Britain held the sea.
Although, however, Gibraltar is of vital
importance to England in many ways, she
does not attempt to advance across the
neutral ground into Spain. She has no wish
to do could not do it if she had. Once
let her leave the shadow of the sheltering
rock for the purpose of an advance into
Andalusia and, unless she advanced with a
force far greater than ever sailed from the
coast of Britain, ultimate defeat and retreat
would be inevitable. But the idea of a
foreign Power’s fixing on a desolate Aus-
tralian promontory and fortifying it, without
being able to proceed any great distance
from the base of operations, and with nothing-
particular to gain by proceeding, is fantastic.
# * *
This is not all. It may be said that,
but for the help given by the French and
Spanish forces (a help balanced by the
assistance afforded by Hessian mercenaries to
England), America would not have attained
independence at the time. Washington,
probably, and Mr. Tom Paine, certainly,
would have been hanged when caught. Still,
no country has since attempted to dispute
her right over a territory larger than Aus-
tralia. Should it be advanced that the
nations of Europe M ere too busy watching
and tearing each other for the 26 years from
the fall of the Bastille to the collapse at
Waterloo to care about the Union and its
ways, it may be answered that in 1814 the
British found their way to Washington, but
took care not to remain there. Again, there
exist in South America, within a couple of
weeks’ sail from Europe, magnificent and
sparsely-populated lands, on whose coasts
European Powers could erect fortresses every
mile with little fear of being disturbed.
Why do they not do so ? Because the Union
would put its foot down with an empathic
“hands off,” it may be answered. This
brings us to the kernel of the matter. In
the event of even immediate federation and
independence, it is wildly improbable that
rtny Power would presume to interfere wit h
Australia’s development in the direction of
a powerful Democracy. But, even if Aus-
tralia failed in the effort to hold her own,
it is almost certain that the great North
American Republic would never tolerate the
subversion of a kindred people, whose only
aim was to work out its own destiny on the
lines laid down by the famous men of a
century ago. Why should Australians seek
for a possible American guarantee while they
have a certain British one already 1 ? The
answers are many, but one will suffice. It
is because connection with an old monarchy
like England, where privilege is still rampant,
and where pinchbeck titles are still revered
by hereditary parasites, has a tendency to
foster a similar state of society here. In
brief, we want to breed here a nation of
men, not of snobs, or, if that is impossible,
to take care that the sycophant is not re-
warded for his fiunkeyism by means of a
foreign and trumpery title. It would pay
Australia better to —using the words of Sir
George Grey —“ loosely federate” with the
United States than with Great Britain.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“ Not Provided For.”
Treasurer M'Millan has snappishly with-
drawn the New South Wales Government
account from the City Bank, on the grounds
that the latter contravened explicit Treasury
instructions by permitting an unauthorised over-
draft to the Department of Public Instruction,
and, later on, obeyed the orders of the Treasury
by dishonoring cheques for which no funds had
been provided. Altogether, 537 cheques were
dishonored, the total amount represented being
£3301 13s. 6d., but the Treasurer confines his
anathemas to the dishonor of two paltry cheques
of £9 *2s. fid, and £5 I7s. respectively. To
rightly assess the blame of this pretty quarrel,
it is necessary to remember a criminal case of
embezzlement by overdraft in which the London
Chartered Bank had to stand a big loss. It is
also requisite to regard dates. On 10th January,
1890, Mr. Geoffrey Eagar, Under-Secretary
of the Treasury, notified, amongst other insti-
tutions, the City Bank, ‘ ‘ that overdraft must
not be allowed on any public account under
any circumstances whatever, except upon the
written authority of the Colonial Treasurer,
conveyed to you in the usual official way.' -
That was 13 months ago. On 19th January,
1891, the Public Instruction Department, hav-
ing no funds whatever to its credit, issued
cheques which the City Bank honored,
and which incurred an overdraft oi
£1256 4s. 7d. The bank immediately
notified the Treasury of this fact. On the 20th
and 21st of January last, more cheques were
presented and honoured, and on the 22nd ult.
the Educational overdraft was £3558 17s. Id.
A bank officer was then despatched to inter-
view Mr. Geoffrey .Eagar personally. Mr.
Eagar severely reprimanded the bank for per-
mitting an overdraft, but said, in reply to a
question, that £5OOO would be paid in to the
credit of the bank that afternoon. That
amount was so paid in, anti lasted until the
24th ult., when the credit again became ex-
hausted. The bank, now thoroughly on its guard,
dishonoured some cheques drawn on the non-
existent account the same tlay. On the 27th
January —or eight days after—the bank re-
ceived a written reply to its first communica-
tion of the 19th. This reply asked for an
explanation respecting the irregularity of having
permitted an overdraft. On the 30th, the
Treasurer formally authorised an overdraft of
£lO,OOO in that particular account. On 3rd
February, the Treasurer notified the bank that
the Government account would be withdrawn,
and emphasised that notification by reitera-
tion on the sth instant. In other words, the
Government lias decided to punish and persecute
a bank for foolishly (or generously) upholding the
public credit without funds and without autho-
rity. The bank no doubt was guilty of a techni-
cal offence in not obeying literally the Treasury
instructions. But the principal blame lay with
the Public Instruction Department, the res-
ponsible head of which either did or did not
know that his account was exhausted. If he
did not know, then he has proved himself an
incompetent and negligent officer; if he did
know, then he wittingly issued valueless
cheques —an offence with a very serious side to
it, in the case of a private person. The bank
showed a fair degree of caution in despatching
an officer to interview Mr. Eagar, but again
they did not literally follow instructions and
obtain “the loritten authority of the Colonial
Treasurer.” That is no small matter, however,
for it is not always an easy matter to get at Mr.
M‘Mxllan or any other Minister. Two necessary
things appear to have been left undone. The
Public Instruction Department should itself
have notified the Treasury that it was hard-up,
and the bank should have warned the Public
Instruction Department as well as the Treasury.
The whole now shows how loosely the public
accounts are kept by the various Government
Departments, and if the bank has erred lightly
in the first degree, the Government has sinned
grievously in the seventh and higher degree,
and is now trying to cover its faults by sham ■
ming a virtuous patriotism for ‘ ‘ public in
terests.” The withdrawing of the Government
account is an unwarrantable act of vindictive
injustice.
H.M. Ship Penguin.
The story of the warship Penguin, now lying
at Hobart, seems to be the old story of the
Egeria over again, with sundry variations for
the worse, the Penguin being apparently the
nether hell of the naval service, while the
Egeria was an upper region of the same deep-
sea Gehenna. The tale, as told by the crew, is
the same as has already been told so often in
British history. The ship was bossed by a
“ rigid disciplinarian,” and life on board
seems to have been one incessant round
of work and punishment, with more work and
more punishment by way of variety. At
Fremantle the crew broke into open mutiny,
and looted the stores, and for a little while it
seems to have been a toss-up whether they did


not make a glorious bonfire of the vessel, and
leave a vacancy in the British navy. One sailor
deserted, and ultimately died of exposure and
starvation ; a lieutenant who attempted to
remonstrate is awaiting trial by court-martial;
and the ship’s company in general seems to have
been divided into two sections—a small party
which was dead, and a large one that wanted
to be. The life of the alleged joyous tar con-
sists, at the best, chiefly of small pay and hard
biscuit; generally it is largely mixed up with
execrations and chains and similar pleasant
facts ; and when he strikes a worse patch than
usual he is practically in hell on a precarious
income. And if he mutinies and is hanged the
chances are that he only loses the income ; the
location, we should imagine, remains about the
same.
The Progressive Gladstone.
The great Parnell-O’Shea riot has left at
least one enduring result behind it —it has
made a woeful smash in Gladstone’s reputa-
tion for political honesty. When that wily
statesman introduced his famous Home Rule
Bill he provided that the Imperial Parliament
should still dictate the fiscal and land policy of
Ireland, and should appoint magistrates and
judges, and retain the entire control of the con-
stabulary, so that the new Irish Parliament
was to be the mere shadow of a shade. But
the measure was accepted as the beginning of
better things. It was the first recognition of
the Irishman’s right to govern himself, and
there was unlimited confidence in the further
expansion of Gladstone’s newly-awakened
Liberalism. Since then the lavish eloquence of
the old politician has led to a firm conviction
that the next Home Rule Bill would provide
for a genuine instead of a bogus system of
autonomy, and this conviction held good until
the Irish party endeavoured to obtain some
definite assurances, and then the ancient
Liberal straightway froze up and refused to
commit himself to anything further than the
abortive measure of five years ago. The com-
plaint of the Irish people is that their manufac-
tures were ruined through the Freetrade system
forced upon them by England, and if England
is to boss the fiscal policy of Ireland there is no
hope of improvement. Also they point out that
their agricultural industry has been destroyed
by the English land system, and if the Irish
Parliament is to have no power to improve the
land system then the people will remain as poor
and as wretched as before. And, in addition to
these grievances, the laws are administered
exclusively in the landlord’s interest by English
Tory magistrates, and the people are harassed
and outraged by a police force maintained solely
for the defence of property-owners, and if this
evil also is to be beyond the sphere of Irish
politics then there is no reason or logic in Home
Rule at all. Apparently Gladstone has not
learned anything new in the past five years,
and if so his Irish policy and that of Salisbury
have no material points of difference; They take
simply two separate ways of making the same
mistake.
• ♦
We have read in the English press by recent
mails at least fifty such protests as this :
At Chester Assizes, Mr. Justice Vaughan Williams sat
from 9.30 a.m. till midnight in order to get through the
calendar and keep his appointment at Caermarthen,
which had already been postponed for two days. The
midnight sitting at Chester wound up with a sentence of
five years’ penal servitude on a woman convicted of per-
jury. That the woman did not deserve it we should be
sorry to say, but that her case was tried with the care
and attention which every prisoner ought to receive is
altogether open to doubt. In saying this we pass no
reproach upon the Judge or any one else concerned. It
is simply inconceivable that men engaged in hard intel-
lectual work for twelve or fourteen hours at a stretch can
keep their-judgment clear and their brains vigorous to
the last; and it stands to reason that when prisoners are
being tried in this fashion against time—Judge, jury, and
counsel all knowing that they cannot get to bed till the
job is done—the business must be hurried and scamped
to a greater or less extent.
Such is the spirit in which a trial of this kind
—involving merely the liberty of one person—
is received by the British press and public.
What, then, would public opinion in
England say of the Mount Rennie case, at
Sydney, in which a week of exhausting sittings,
all devoted to this one case, was wound up by
(he Court sitting right on from Friday morning
at 0 o'clock until Saturday morning at d.JO, and
then n mming business at 9 undock same morning
—to sit right on till half -au-honr before midnight,
at which time nine out of eleven youths were
sentenced to death, four of them being subse-
quently executed with the concurrence of Baron
Cakington, whose governorship of New South
Wales has been pronounced, by people who
know nothing about it, to have been so howling
a success? When is justice to lie done to the
surviving remnant of these miserables—and to
Sweetman, victim of a savage and unjust
sentence ?
“ Thermidor,” by Sardou, has been pro*
hibited in Paris because of its anti-Robespierre-
ism. Now, Sydney sillies rise to remark that
this proves that democracy is unlimited
tyranny. As a matter of fact, the play is
prohibited in the interests of peace and human
life—because the admirers of the Montagnard
have threatened to wreck any theatre where
it may bo played, and have already come to
blows with the descendants of the Girondists
and Royalists, to whom Robes pier he’s name is
as a red rag. Putting that aside, to make a
parallel —would the Pahkes (lev eminent permit
anyone to produce a play reflecting on the
morality of Queen Victoria, or to march down
the back slums playing “ Boyne Water,” or
“ Croppies Lie Down ? ” Yet the refusals would
not be styled tyrannical by the papers alluded
to.
The London Times wants to know what is the
good of creating an Irish Parliament in Dublin,
considering what an awful wreck of imbecility
and grovel and rowdyism and incapacity the
Irish Parliament used to be when it howled on
College Green away back in the last century,
and allowed itself on an average the meanest
legislative assembly in Europe. Looking back
on that Irish Parliament, it certainly was a
remarkable gathering, and taking it all round
it would have sold its country or its father for
a sixpence. But as no Catholic was allowed to
uite in these good old days, any more than any
Catholic was allowed to sit in the House, so it fol-
lowsthat thatghastly Legislature represented the
Times’s own party and no other, and it was
made up of Abigail’s ancestors and the prede-

bigots generally. The complaint that the Irish
nation is not fit for self-government comes with
rather a bad grace from the handful of Ulster

the story of that old Parliament, if it shows

A Melbourne anti-vaccinationist, one of whose
children had suffered grievously from the after-
effects of vaccination, has been proceeded
against for refusing to run the risk of thus
inflicting life-suffering on another of his little
ones. When fined, the defendant pathetically
asked, “If Tgoto gaol will I save my child? I don’t
mind that to save my little one from a dread-
ful risk.” Now, there seems to be little doubt
that vaccination is a preventive of smallpox;
there is also little doubt that it is now and then
the means of communicating blood-poisoning
and glandular diseases. Taking all into con-
sideration, it is not evident that the Govern-
ment of any country are wise in forcing parents
to subject their children to an operation only
of problematical value while of possible hurt.
The writer has never forgotten the fiery diatribe,
accentuated by a wealth of regret for the lost
one, that Gerald Massey launched at the
officialism that doomed his only child to fade
away beneath his eyes, the victim of a loath-
some disease caused by vaccination.
That little podgy nobleman, the Duke of
Argyle, writes thus upon the agitation for
Home Rule in Scotland :—-
I never hear a word about Home Rule for Scotland,
except the report of a few foolish speeches in the papers.
As we don’t know what Home Rule means even for Ire-
land, there is no use in discussing-that enigmatical phrase
as applied to Scotland. All the wealth and prosperity of
Scotland dates from the Union, and it would be putting
back the clock indeed to go back to our former state of
“ single blessedness,” which was one of great poverty and
frequent distress.
Just so. Also the wealth and prosperity of
America date from the hanging of Major Andre,
and the fall of Holland dates from the great fire
of London, but it doesn’t follow that there is
any connection between these events, all the
same. Further, the Kelly Gang date from
about the time of the City of Glasgow Bank
failure, and half-a-dozen revolutions broke out
in Europe almost immediately after the first
bishop arrived in Melbourne, and the Duke of
Argyle himself probably dates from the time
when somebody was hanged in Siam. When a
duke starts to draw an inference something is
certain to go wrong.

The Gentlewoman, a large London publication
which panders to the female passion for grovel,
lately went into hysterics over those two puerile
shadows of French royalty, the Comte and
Comtesse de Paris. Although the second-hand
pretenders are playing their old family farce in
England, “it is a significant fact,” chirps the
Gentlewoman, “ that the mansion is only
hired, reminding you that the Comte de Paris
regards himself as but a sojourner in a foreign
land. And although the time of exile may be
weary waiting, there is comfort in the thought
that there is a tide in the affairs of men, &c.,
and, as the Comte has himself declared,
‘ Uheure venue je serai pret. ’ ” There is precious
little comfort in the thought that such greasy
balderdash is enjoyed by a number, however
small, of British matrons, who hang on to the
vague idea that this fatuous French loafer will
one day be sent for to “save his country.” No
other kind of Tory journalism is so shamelessly
snobbish and hopelessly stupid as the stuff pre-
pared for “ ladies ” only. Yet it fills, pre-
sumably, the usual long-felt want. And we
Radicals, who rejoice to find the great majority
of mankind turning their backs upon the effete
monarchical idol, must still recognise the poison-
ous existence of people that write—“ You
should certainly make a loyal effort to see the
temple of Concord and Victory ” (in the grounds
adjoining the Comte de Paris’ “ royal ” abode)
“if only for the reason that Queen Victoria
planted the oaks, and the late Prince Consort
the two cedars in front of it.” Booh ! In the
meantime we will make a loyal effort not to be
sick.

Apropos to Florence St. John’s libel action
against a paper which said her jewels were
stolen for advertising purposes, or something
to that effect, “ Oak ados,” of London Referee,
writes : -
Critics, according to certain members of the profession,
are all very well while they slaver and butter and grovel
and adulate and show they know how to “ keep their
place ”in presence of their superiors. They become the
worst and vilest and cruellest creatures in the world
directly they dare move in another direction. As the
majority of critics are quite satisfied to accept the posi-
tion, and to eat dirt when desired by the so-called heads
of the dramatic profession, there is no reason why 1
should step forth and defend them. . . . Therefore it
will suffice for me to say, as I have often said before, that
I am utterly unable to understand upon what logical
grounds actors and actresses suck in praise just as a cat
sucks in cream, and then rush off to the law-courts
directly anything appears in a paper that is contrary to
their living.
The Bulletin would suggest that logical
reasoning is not to be expected of theatrical
folk, the majority of whom are so blinded by
egotism, and so professionally petty-minded
as to he incapable of attributing an unfavour-
able criticism, or blunt comment, to honest
motives. The writer who merely puts into
his own words, as a matter of business,
the true sense of what the majority of
the public happens to be saying, never hopes to
get thought other than a vindictive fool by
many players and some managers who believe
(and purr over) everything written for journals
which instruct their critics either to lie like
tombstones for the sake of the advertisement,
or to “let them down easy” so that no irate
mummers may come bothering with complaints
at the office, or for journals which haven’t a man
on the staff competent to report shows and
keep his right hand unfettered by theatrical
friendships or acquaintances while so doing.
But although the grovel of the orthodox Press
is at times very disgusting, it must be admitted
that there are extenuating circumstances in
■certain cases. The representative of a highly -
influential daily newspaper who could invariably
avoid being “ squared ” with soft.words, cigars,
tender glances, strong drinks, or more weighty
arguments, has not, probably, been born up to
now. Suppose an actress, a tall, large-eyed,
elegantly-appointed creature—gets an introduc-
tion to him, and whispers “How unkind you
were in your last notice.” Is it in human
nature for the mouthpiece of public opinion to
go home and say, even in the most veiled lan-
guage, that the aforesaid actress has no more
talent than a potato-famine ?
Ik blissful ignorance that the ArGus has offi-
cially fainted over the Parnell “ disclosures,”
the London Social Gossiper of that anti-Irish
organ reckons up the situation as follows :
A saturnine bachelor has formed a liaison with a
flighty and attractive woman of his own aye, and the
thing has got into the papers. There are dozens of such
affairs known and recognised in society and the world at
large. One or two other political leaders are in similar
chains, as are many leading admirals, generals, officials,
artists, actors, peers, millionaires, and Q.O.’s. The man
in such cases is prima facie an object of men’s sympathy.
How did he get entangled?—will he ever get free?—such
are the questions to which his condition gives rise. But
on the great outside public the effect in this case has been
far otherwise. The outside public never learns anything.
Year, after year it goes through its course of Divorce
Court shocks, but it knows no more of human nature in
its old age than it did in its childhood.
There is no pretence of high morality in the
sentiments of this accomplished prattler. He is
merely a man of the world talking the common-
sense to which his employer (the Argus) has
not sufficient honesty or pluck to give expres-
sion. It is refreshing to find the Gossiper tell-
ing how—
Day after day, man after man arose to repudiate Mr.
Parnell and all his works. Some of them were worthy
and religious men enough, but there were joined unto
them many unworthy fellows—one, for example, a
drunken and immoral buffoon ; another a man of fashion
just released after many agonising throes from a like
liaison with a Scotch peeress, &c., &c.—such a,procession
of harefaced hypocrites as never before crossed the ivorld’s
stage.
And at the end of this procession the Argus
had—unknown to its own faithful Gossiper—
been flaunting its wretched “ respectable ” little
banner in the breeze created by the flapping of
the wings of Hypocrisy over the soft, addled
head of Ignorance. Every now and then a
fresh, luxuriant joke may be plucked from the
stony ground of dull daily journalism.
The Daily Northern Argus (Rockhampton)
has discovered that Lord Carington “has not
been Governor of the parent colony of Aus-
tralia for nothing ; and that the frequent and
pungent criticisms of The Bulletin upon his
ability to take an intelligent view of political
questions affecting Australia were considerably
overdone—overdone to malice, for the only
reason that the subject of them—shall we say
it?—was a peer of the realm.” The D.N.A.
has suddenly evolved a veneration for Caring-
ton, because the latter was “ plucky enough ”
to say ‘ ‘ before the future King of England ”
“ that the colonies don’t want and will resent
English interference in their affairs, and that
they consider that Imperial Federation is un-
workable.” The D.N.A. is correct within
narrow limits, but fails to become seized of all
the- facts connected with Carington’s conver-
sion. It is no vain boastfulness, but a desire
to be truthful, that leads us to point out
that it is from The Bulletin that Carington
received his education on Australian aspira-
tions and anti-imperialistic sentiments —that,
in short, The Bulletin and the now strong
party whom it has inspired, converted Caring-
ton, turning him from the darkness of Tummy-
dom into the light of free self-government.
Even the D.N.A., how'ever, can’t “abide”
Cakington’s proposal to create an Australian
peerage, so that The Bulletin’s “ frequent
and pungent criticisms ” were rather underdone
than “ considerably overdone.” The Bulletin
has no special ‘ ‘ malice ” against Carington—
it merely loathes the sight of a titled nobody
from afar off with a reputation for wealth and
Royal favour and a lot of racecourse and society
cunning as his stock-in-trade, placed in the
chair which should be filled, amid the acclaim
of the Australian nation, by aii able and
patriotic Australian.
Dear Bulletin. —In an article of the 24th
ult. you deal with Australia’s borrowing, and
adduce cogent reasons for discontinuing the
practice, but have overlooked the reason most
likely to weigh with the present generation,
viz., that the present effect of the expenditure
of borrowed money by any country must be to
reduce the amount of ready money in that
country; e.<j. , Gillies and Co, spent in five
years thirteen millions of foreign capital. On
what? Permanent improvements. It required
labor of the value say of €7,000,U00 to effect
these improvements, and consequently labour
of that value was withdrawn from avenues of
production. These labourers were paid market
rates, and would not have starved had no loan
monies been expended. It appears, therefore,
(hat the exchangeable values of the country
were reduced by a sum equal to the earnings of
that labour under nominal conditions, say
£7,000,000. If this view be correct, and gain
acceptance wdth the public generally, it will do
more to check our extravagant loan expenditure
than the most convincing arguments founded on
any actual or supposed injury to posterity. I
have refrained from discussing the values of
these permanent improvements, as that does not
affect my argument However valuable these
may be, they do not represent ready money or
exchangeable values.—C.W.C.
—.— *
Though the Federation of Australia has not
yet begun, the people of Albury have again
started to agitate for the appointment of their
town as the federal capital, and Parkes, it is
hardly necessary to mention, agrees that the
little settlement on the New South Wales
border must be the political centre of United
Australia. Everybody who knows Parkes
must have foreseen that the old man would
discover some place in his own province to be
the only possible capital of the Federation, and
if the other provinces fail to see it, he will pro-
bably back out more in sorrow than in anger,
and tell in after years that be and 29 other
men had two opinions between them, and if tha
other 28 hadn’t been so ignorant and so miser-
ably obstinate all might have been well. Tha
only disadvantage about Albury, regarded as
the central point of the new State, is that it
isn’t in the centre of anything, and that it is at
the far south-eastern corner of everything, and
its one decided advantage is that Parkes
wants it, and if Parkes climbs on a chair and
howls for the moon he must have it, or else
break something. It is about as suitable as a
dozen other towns, and that is all. In any case
it is an ominous sign that New South Wales
should have started to scramble so very early
for the chance of getting ahead of the other
Australias. The object of Federation is the
general good of Australia, and not the separate
advantage of any particular State, and even if
tire capital should be ultimately planted out in
the great Australian desert, no one has any
just cause to complain. But we are surprised
at the silence of the Wentworthites, the old
disputers of Albury folks’ claim to the title of
“ Federal Capital.”

Arthur Rae, shearers’ delegate, says that the
squatters’ new agreement gives each individual
squatter power to make and to enforce what-
ever additional regulations he chooses to impose
on his shed slaves. The Bulletin notes also
that the agreement proposes to compel the
“general hand” to “continue working from
day to day till the whole of the sheep and
lambs ” are shorn, thus robbing him of his
right to leave after a week’s notice, while it
permits the master to “ sack ” the man at any
time. Thus, a “white-headed boy” of the
overseer may come along looking for a job, and
the overseer may immediately find fault with
and discharge a hand to put on his favourite.
Under the existing rules, no member of the
general labourers’ union can take the place of
another member so discharged, and there is an
unwritten law prohibiting a member camping
at any station to wait for such a “ death.” If
a member can’t be put on at once, he must
travel further.
We have amongst us so many filthy whites that
we cannot afford to welcome to Australia the East
Indian, our fellow-citizen under the glorious
British flag. We must deal with him as we
have dealt with our beloved fellow-citizen the
Hongkong Chinaman. The Indians are terrors
to unprotected women and children, whom
they always contrive to interview when the
males of the family are abroad. The city dens
that shelter these spindle-shanked niggers are
almost invariably lazar-houses of filth, vermin,
and infamy. At Sydney Police Court last week
Mahomet Something was fined CIO for keeping
filthy premises. In five rooms he herded 40
nigs. In one room, lying on a vermin-infested
mattress on the floor, were one woman and two
girls, “stark naked and covered with hideous
sores.” The house reeked with stenches from
putrefying animal and vegetable refuse which
littered the floors, and the back-yard was a bog
of heterogeneous corruption. Yet this was the
place where sauces were made, to be hawked
round town. Just fancy eating roast fowl
stuffed by a Chinese cook and served up with
Mahomet sauce !
Thus the principal leading idiot of the Austra-
lasian :
One of the functions, for instance, which the demo-
cratic spirit claims as its own is to prove that there is no
such thing as superiority, and no such beings as superior
persons. Acting on religions it resolves the gods into
solar myths, and proves that the priests and prophets
and saints of the hagiologies either never existed, or
were grossly overrated persons. In politics it shows that
the theory of the divine right of Kings was simply a sac-
religious invention of successful robbers, and it prefers as
its Parliamentary representatives not men of light and
learning, but servile delegates with capacity for dis-
sembling and for repeating the clamours and shibboleths
of the hour. In the household the father has ceased to be
priest and king, and his patriarchal despotism has been
changed into a very limited monarchy, in which a strong
public opinion exists in favour of *“ sparing the rod.”
Children no longer stand uncovered in their parents’
presence till bidden to be seated, and no longer address
them ceremoniously as “ Sir ”or “ Madam.” Amid such
changes it would be impossible for the relation of master
and scholar to be unaffected, and accordingly it is melan-
choly to think how degenerate a being the schoolmaster
of to-day is compared with the Plagosus Orbilius of
Horace, or the awful Dr. Busby, who refused a bishopric
because he had flogged every bishop on the bench.
The Bulletin hereupon gets tip to ask the
Australasian one question : What is that old
hunk of print driving at ? Does it claim that
“the gods” are not myths, and is it ready to
worship the eat and the onion and the stuffed
monkey and the leek, and to become a Druid,
and to burn incense and whoop in a naked state
on the mountain tops, after the fashion that
existed in the old ante-democratic days, and if
it isn’t, then what is it howling about? Is it
prepared to maintain the “divine right of
Kings,” and to claim that William of Nor-
mandy was the special servant of the Lord
because he successfully killed his predecessor,
and cut off the hands and feet and burned out
the eyes of his predecessor’s followers ; and that
Richard 111. was divinely appointed because
he murdered his nephew; and that Victoria
reigns by the grace of Rod because the venal
Welsh member whose vote decided that the
house of Guelph should rule in place of the
house of Stuart broke his horse’s neck on the
road to London instead of breaking his own ;
and if not what is the row about ? Or again,
supposing the Orange plot of 1885 had suc-
ceeded, and Victoria had been ejected or other-
wise “ disposed of ” to make May for the Duke
of Cumberland, would he also have possessed
the “ divine right of Kings,” and in that case
does divine right mean the same as robbery with
violence? Also, when this apostle of brutality
wails because the old flogging days are over,
does it wail because it wants to flog somebody
else—say a woman or a child—or to be flogged
itself; and if any of these ideas is the case, why
doesn’t it state the fact openly, or, on the other
hand, if neither is the case, what is the cause
of the unearthly disturbance ? If the Austra-
lasian ever offered a coherent explanation in
its life would it kindly offer one now, and ex-
plain what it wants.
Count Herisaon, in his “ Memoirs of
Napoleon III,” denies that the surrendorer of
Sedan died under the operation of lithotrity, but
attributes his death to an overdose of chloral
administered in spite of his violent antipathy to
that sedative.

SIC Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Feb. 11, IROI
My Dear Moorabinda. —
Though Lord Jersey may not be stylish enough to
frame, ho makes amends for it in his staff, which is rather
a relief after the baggy, puffy, taciturn, odd-sized and
bald-headed following of the other idol. The Earl of
Ancrura (by the by, the sun must have got into my eyes
when I accused him of having red hair) is a charming
youth with an interesting pallor and a small dark
moustache—the realised dream of any .boarding-school;
brother Rupert No. 2, “Chumley,” and Goschen are
generally beautiful to gaze on, but their chief attraction
is their youth and kittenishness. One has no fear lest
their joints should stiffen when they stand on the damp
grass, and you could go deeply into the gout question
before the whole crowd of them without embarrassing a
single aide-de-camp. Secretary Goschen, by the way, is
rather too skittish for some of the poker-backed members
of a Sydney club, for lie sat on a table in the sacred pre-
cincts the other evening, I am told, and strung his heels
while the strict etiquette-ridden raembahs present gazed
at him in a stony way and failed to see the airy cama-
raderie of the thing.
Lord Carington was once guilty of a still more un-
dignified perch—at the Camden Hospital opening. lie
was welcoming the first patients to that worthy institu-
tion, and he did so roosting with pendulating feet on the
edge of an umbrella-stand !!! But then any number of
young gentlemen—heirs to big softgoods shops—will tell
you in a horrified whisper that “ Carington really wasn't
quite form, dontcherknow.”
We are fast becoming familiarized with the unassuming
Lord Jersey. We are getting to know the misfit, funnel-
shaped beaver, the long-waisted frock coat, themuliebrous
smile and the bulgy umbrella which appertain to the
brand-new Governor when he hasn’t his cocked hat and
trimmings on. People have become inured to Jersey in
a surprisingly short time. At the opening of the big
tomato and fat-horse show at Parramatta lie went the
rounds with only two of the committee to do \vorship t
and even the children of the forest and the dazed Being
with the clay of the orange orchard on his bluchers didn't
think it worth while to troop after him from stall to stall
with their mouths open in the good old way, but let him
amble by without even turning sometimes.
The so-called “ improper story ” which caused so much
trouble at the Union Club the other day was a very harm-
less thing after all—that is to say if I am to regard as
authentic a version supplied to me by a married woman
of my acquaintance. In fact, there was no story at all—
the cause of the fuss consisting in a very outspoken
remark, which was thought so little of that the lady con-
cerned conveyed it to her fiery husband by way of
anecdote, and not by way of complaint.
Speaking of Clubs, I hear that a recently-returned
member of one of these intends to try and introduce the
custom observed in all really‘swagger British clubs, of
having ail change washed before it is given to a member.
The notion is hygienic, anyhow. Some of the money
given in the clubs must have come from the “ vegetable
John,” who usually counts his change into his mouth us
an extra precaution. And then just consider the un-
cleanliness of the methods by which so many of the mag-
nates of the land get their money !
On dit that the recent tour of a toney concert company
in a hilly and windy land was of a decidedly gay and
festive character. One excursion was taken in a tandem
which was rushed along at such a “ bat ” that the leader
was left for dead on the roadside, and the shaft-horse
staggered in on three legs. All this happened just after
the lunch.
I hear of a couple of impending divorce-suits, the
defendant being in each case a Benedict who has dis-
tinguished himself witli a lady introduced to help in the
household machinery. When ladies are introduced to
help in the household machinery, they very often do
help in it—in fact, 1 have come to regard lady-helps as
women who help themselves to the mistress's shoes. The
psychologically - interesting part of one of these little
skirmishes off the path of duty is that the legal charmer
is ever so much more attractive than (he tootsy-
wootsy : the prospective plaintiff is a really taking
woman, while the coining co respondent has a shape that
suggests empty reels of cotton on wires, a bilious eye
and a set of yellow teetli that would do credit to a horse.
“ Variety is the spice of life,” of course, but it is difficult
to understand why some people leave peaches and cream
for crali apples and vinegar.
The question that is alleged to be agitating half the
social world at present is whether the appropriated
angel ought to visit the theatre with any other man but
her husband, or her grandfather, or somebody else
equally harmless. The consensus of public opinion seems
to be in the negative. But why should not the neglected
sposa of the gentleman who is too bored to run her out
o •casionally, be allowed the brief boon of spending an
Lour or two in a plush chair beside a male friend
of the family? But thete are some decollete
matrons (I have two or three in my mind’s eye,
Horatio) of the “ I-want-to-be-a-widow ” variety, who
carl their chemicallocks on top o( their dyed eyebrows
and are as instructive as an anatomy seance, to whom
this freedom sjiould not be given. If a friendly man
takes one of them anywhere—out of kindness, or to pay
asocial debt—she immediately concludes that he is des-
perately in love with her, and lets her stiffened locks
tickle ins fear as she leans towards him at a painful slant.
For matrons of this variety (lie whole race of men, bar
ing their ow n legal property, should be labelled “ pizen.”
It is an unnatural sight to see how a leading man at n
Sydney theatre takes hold of the leading lady in the
most impressive love scenes, as if he were frightened some
long, deadly pin in her make-up might lose its grip and
bring about a dire catastrophe. When he strokes his
darling’s hair his caressing palm is quite four inches off
her cranial doormat, and the wooden way in which the
clinging arms of love are adjusted about her willowy form
smacks loudly an inch off the lobe of her shelly ear!
I he canoodling right through is so mechanical that one
almost yearns for a touch of the bounding passion of the
Potter, who hurled herself with a shriek at the man of
her choice and rather preferred to bow her acknowledge-
ments with her back hair in her mouth.
Quite the latest excitement in a large and pious circle
is the “ China Inland Mission,” in respect to which ever
so many young men and women hear the Divine voice
calling them to the region of easy divorces and almond
eyes. It’s a lovely idea—the Mission-for the he or she
who has a fancy for a turn round a strange land and no
objection to travel under the most Arcadian conditions,
and withal in an odour of sanctity. I am much grieved,
however, to read in a pamphlet on China that “though
there are earnest men and women amongst the foreign
missionaries, the misdeeds of a few have brought the
body as a class into disrepute.” If I were a ma, and a
daughter of mine wanted to go and convert the Chinese
in company with a lot of young men and women who
proposed going to China in the same ship, I am sure I
should insist on one of the young men marrying my girl
before she left Australia. That is all I have to say on the
subject at present, beyond remarking that people who are
very pious are always dreadfully affectionate.
The She-Miss'.onary is always more or less equivocal
even when she hasn’t fallen “as a class” into actual dis-
repute, and the best of her generally keeps a spicy record
of her “ experiences,” and one or two of the leading pro-
perties of the country in her Gladstone bag ; after which
she invariably secures, in her pious innocence, the best
show-agent disengaged and launches out in the paying
churchy-show business “ under the patronage of the
Primate.” The lady disciple in the show biz. has always
commanded my wondering admiration for the worldly
way she can gather up a treasury every Saturday, and
the far-off, dreamy manner in which she gets into a pair
of pants on a fifteen-feet stage, because they are the
correct wear for ladies in the Barbarian land she carries
about with her.
Woman always has been a weird anomaly, and she
doesn’t mean to alter awhile. For instance, the Cruelty
to Animals Society in Sydney is principally composed of
energetic ladies, who proudly wear its medals on their
bosoms, and start into eager life if a cabby cracks his
whip. And yet recently I was in an omnibus with one of
these Avengers, when on a steep hill a passenger stopped
and alighted. On jerked the poor horses again for about
a yard, when the Dumb Animals’ Friend hung on the
strap to be let out, and another hard pull resulted. It
was very evident her humanity didn’t include tracking a
yard uphill on her philanthropic tootsy-wootsy.
The Goulburn Cathedral squabble, chapter 2, will pro-
bably end in favour of Captain Rossi, whose love of
scarlet neckties and reputation for breeding prize pug-
dogs don’t seem to interfere with his religious convic-
tions. Now, if the Captain (he is, or was, a captain of
volunteers) bred prize bull-dogs and wore red neckties he
couldn’t possibly be a good man.
1 am told that on the evening of the great Federal
Convention banquet on 2nd March in Sydney Centennial
Hall womankind will be allowed into the galleries to
focus its hungry gaze upon the disappearing ham.
Principally under the safe chaperonage of Secretary
Wellington, the Ladies Hope, sisters of the Victorian
Governor, did the Blue Mountains as far as Katoomba
last week, stopping at the “ Carrington.” They were
greatly impressed by the bounding untamed waratah in
its native lair, and toiled about after the lions with the
enthusiasm of the ordinary untitled she who revels in an
excursion ticket. A future jaunt of the ladies will be to
the Jenolan Caves. By the way, there was a half-
expressed opinion amongst local sassiety that the newly-
arrived “ scionesses ” of nobility didn't get quite as much
change out of their lordly prefix, so to speak, as any
newly-made colonial “lady” does out of hers, and one
leading Sydney society person who dreams of the time
when she can assume the frills that appertain to a gilded
“handle,” plaintively remarked that “The dear things
weren’t quite reserved enough—not for titled women, my
dear.”
The successful amateur athlete of to-day is apt to
develope into a nuisance in his family circle—unless that
circle is stripped of the conveniences of life, and eats its
mutton off tin plates. He fills the house with his
guerdons—with coffee-pots and beer-jugs, and urns and
mugs, and epergnes and biscuit-holders, when there were
quite enough before, and in this day of ninc-carat
“ gold,” and bewitched tin masquerading as solid silver,
the housewife who prides herself on her side-boards often
verges on tears as she sees the loom of the brass on the
lid of the trophy tea-pot, and daren’t relegate it to the
kitchen or the dust-box for fear of hurting dear Harry’s
feelings. The “ girls ” in one family which suffers under
an amateur who returns every Saturday in the season
with some new aggravation in silver frost and solder,
have to turn to every week and scour up the gathering
trophies which the housemaid refuses to “do.” “And
it’s spoiling our hands for nothing,” complained one
recently, “ for George won’t have them used, and when
by chance he wins a bangle or anything useful to us he
doesn’t even bring it home but gives it away to some girl
or other.”

Quite a festive air was lent to the Garrick on 3rd inst.
by Mrs. Burdekin’s octagonal theatre party—four ladies
and four gentlemen. Mrs. Burdekin wore heliotrope silk
veiled in thick, cream lace, the bodice having zouaves of
the silk, cluster of pale-pink roses and ferns on the left
shoulder and pearl and moonstone necklace ; Miss Bur-
dekin wore elephant’s breath grey voile with grey velvet
belt, en Suisse, the corsage softened by pale-blue chiffon
frills ; Miss Broomfield was in grey brocade and net and
scarlet fan, and poppies on the shoulder ; the other lady
in the party in bright rose-pink voile and silk with
Empire sash of spring green. In the circle a recent bride
was decolette in scarlet silk ; a society matron not exactly
of a fasting habit wore a black bodice beaded all over and
relieved with a frontal scarf of pink chiffon ; and a bean-
pole damsel, with Jersey hair dressed A la sky-terrier,
waved her elbows wildly about on top of the velvet-
stuffed balustrade as if she was carving a tough turkey.
The Earl of Jersey goes to Penrith on 14th inst. (as a
sort of vice-regal Valentine) to turn on the new water-
works in the capital of the Nepean. The usual alleged
rejoicings will take place, after which the Earl will
return to Sydney.
A party of folks fresh from England who had just
returned from a trip to the Mountains were gushing over
the beautiful scenery when one of them ecstatically
exclaimed; “But, oh! the beef at Katoomba! I don’t
think I have ever tasted such meat since I left England.
Why, the undercut was just like a solid mouthful of
gravy.” Oh 1 those awful English women 1 I wonder how
many of them allow their love of the beautiful to exceed
their anxiety for their creature comforts. I don’t think
I shall ever again view the natural beauties of the azure
mountains without thinking of undercut and gravy.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH
Over fifty frolicsome juveniles assembled at “ Caris-
brooke,” Waverley, the residence of Mr. R. G. Watkins,
on 27th ult.— the date of the birthday of their eldest
daughter, Miss Maude Watkins, who was the little hostess
of the festivities, which passed off very merrily. Many
pretty frocks were worn by the small guests.
On the evening of the 4th inst., the Mayor of Sydney
and Mrs. Burdekin gave a successful gipsying up Middle
Harbour in the Government steam launch “ Premier,”
with Mr. Shaw Stewart, M.P., and Lady Shaw Stewart
(who have now gone on to China), as the lions of the
occasion ; Captain “ Chumley ” and Mr. Goschen were
also present. Dancing to a good string band was the
amusement after “ high tea” had been discussed, and a
start for town was made at 10 p.m.
The engagement of Miss Amy Noyes, daughter of Dr.
Noyes, of Deniliquin, N.S.W., to Mr. Andrew Brown,
second son of the late Mr. “Tom” Brown, of Tuppal
Station, on the Murray, is publicly announced.
Governor Jersey has promised to entertain the Rand-
wick Industrial School boys and girls at Government
House one day during the winter, when the long-looked-
for Countess will be there to superintend the buns and
help him in speech-making.
On 6th inst. St. Mary’s Pro-Cathedral, Sydney, was well
patronised by nursemaids and their charges, attracted
thither by the quiet ceremonies attendant on the mar-
riage of Miss Kelly, daughter of Mr. T. Kelly, of By rock
station, Bourke, to Mr. Francis Dillon Bell, eldest son of
the Agent-General for New Zealand. The bridal gown
was of ivory faille Francaise, chiffon and orange blossoms,
with diamond swallow brooch (a gift from papa), the one
bridesmaid also wearing white, with gauze hat trimmed
with ostrich feathers. The wedding breakfast was served
at the Hotel Metropole, after which Mr. and Mrs. Dillon
Bell left for the honeymoon, the bride travelling in cocoa-
brown cloth trimmed with silk designed in old blue, small
hat en mite.

standard quality that can be obtained in regis-

Uam-Var in special keg or bottle, and see that
you get it.

“How on earth does he'manage it?”
That is a question we have often heard asked
concerning men who on small salaries gave
rekerky entertainments. Those men, we have
found, always “did it ”in one way—a way which
ultimately meant skipping to Honolulu in an
unobtrusive coal-ship or being taken to prison by
the ear.
As Mr. Traill, M.L.A., points out, Post-
master-General O'Connor has committed a gross
act of injustice in impounding certain copies of
the Sydney Referee, which did not comply with
the provisions of the N.S.W. Postal Act, whilst
allosving two flagrant, because deliberate, offences
against the Act by the powerful Sydney Mail —
i.c., the S.M. Herald— proprietary, to pass un-
noticed. Mr. O’Connor may have been technically
right in refusing the Referee transmission on
tins occasion, though the paper’s offence was
trivial and obviously inadvertent, but his action
in making a distinction between the two papers
is more worthy of Russia or of Ireland than of
Australia.
A London gardener was recently fined for
driving to market in an unlicensed cart. When the
magistrate heard that the man’s wife and child had
also been in the cart, he recalled the case and told
the poor gardener that he had rendered himself
criminally liable to a term of imprisonment.
“ How were my wife and child to do the journey?”
urged this audacious rascal. “Go in some other
vehicle or walk,” replied the beak. Another case
was that of a costermonger who used his licensed
coster’s barrow to drive his family to see some
races. The Bench held that by thus converting
his licensed barrow into a family equipage he had
transformed it into a carriage, and the coster was
duly fined, in accordance with law.

Writing hurriedly of “Arrah-na-Pogue”
last week, the name of Grattan Riggs was
entirely omitted. The slight was unintentional,
“ Arrah ” without Riggs would resemble water
without whisky. “The Irish Detective,” a wild
phantasmagoria that every one has seen and
revelled in, was set up at Sydney Royal before a
big audience on Saturday night. The evergreen
Grattan was followed through the seven Riggs-out
that he assumes so faithfully in costume, dialect,
gesture, and every other particular, with keen
interest and howls of laughter. Robt. Inman
was a capital Hoyt, and Capt. de Burgh played
Richard Stockpot, the appropriately named gentle-
men who eventually gets into the soup, with a
ponderous unctuousness that suggested “turtle.’
The Jim Thug of Chas. Seagrave is a clever
impersonation, perhaps more on the lines of Seven
Dials than those of the Bowery. Dick Stewart
and A. G. Boulton are both amusing in different
ways, but the ladies, all round, are disappointing,
and Maud Appleton failed to deepen the impres
sion made by her Arrah. “Eileen Oge,” an
Irish drama, by Edmund Falconer (we saw it in
the English provinces in 72, we think, with
O'Grady in the lead), will be staged on Saturday
night. Next week, it is worthy of note, there
will be three Irish dramas running in Sydney,
two by Boucicault.

There is some pretty writing in “Com-
rades,” but it is based on and bolstered up by im-
probabilities, and, therefore, ill-calculated to
stand the wear and tear of a long run. Dot
Boucicault is particularly fine as Stirrup, the
veteran soldier, and little fault can be found with
the acting of Geo. Titheradge, Arthur Laui’ence,
Miss Romer and Mrs. Brough, while Owen
Harris’ make-up as the {esthete is a marvel, but
Percy Lyndal is too aggravatingly cool and
sweetly pretty. Frank Cates is all right as an
elderly masher, but he can’t look at heavy, old
men, he lacks the breadth, repose and weight
necessary, and plays with a thin-edged jerki-
ness that cuts off sympathy and tells
every girl in front that he’s single. There
is a delicious bit of love-making between Mrs
Brough and Titheradge, followed by proposal and
acceptance that fascinated the house the other
evening. But the lady retreated w ithout “ sealing
the engagement,” and a profound gloom settled
over the powdered bosom of the circle, till she
paused, leftcentre, and looked backwith eyesofin-
vitation. A soul-satisfying, heart-tingling “cheep”
was presently heard and, just as the dowagers
were about to settle themselves contentedly, a
little lady said in a clear, innocent voice, “Oh,
wasn’t that nice?” and then everybody laughed
and liked her for saying it, because she just voiced
the unspoken thought of the entire audience,
“ The Jilt,” a five-act Irish play by the late
Dion Boucicault, will be revived on Saturday
night.

“ The Icuer ” improves on acquaintance.
The author has culled his inspiration from various
sources. For instance, when Strong proposes to
Kate, she affects to be nonplussed and asks him
what she should say. Whereupon he chirps out —
“Don’t know ; had no experience, but if you were
to say ‘Yes, Simeon,’and let me kiss you, I’d
reckon it a good start.” Everyman is familiar
with the yarn from which this speech is adapted.
Throughout, expressions crop up reminiscent of
digging days, and some of its most epigram-
matic lines, for instance, “Failure is the un-
pardonable sin,” are familiar as copy-book
headings. The third act is the brightest
and most vigorous, yet the most highly
finished. It is full of finely-conceived, well-
worked-out scenes. That where Cross induces
Strong to write the letter exonerating Harding is
acted for all it’s worth on both sides, and is fol-
lowed by a strong situation, in which Helen’s
appeal to Cross’s better nature is something to be
remembered, but the act is not then let down, for
the encounter between Harding and the would-be
seducer of his wife fully sustains the interest. As
the Idler, Charles Cartwright has a strained and
incoherent character to handle, one that is right
against human sympathy, and doesn’t fit him, and
in which the measuredness of his intonation is
more unpleasantly apparent than in his previous
Sydney appearances; yet, no one can fail to be
impressed with the ease, power, and finish of the
impersonation. As for Olga Nethersole, her
pathos has the true ring, and strikes a responsive
chord every time, but the part, as Lily Langtry
foresaw, w r ants writing up before it comes within
the category of “star’’parts. “The Idler” will
be withdrawn in the bloom of success in order
that the company may have a fortnight’s Mel-
bourne rehearsal of “The Fortune of War.”
Annis Montague and Charles Turner, after an
absence from the Sydney stage of seven years,
appear on Saturday in “Maritana,” one of their
former triumphs. They will be followed some
time in March by Johnny Sheridan, who will be
ousted by Janet Achurch on the 7th of May.

“ Wild America ” is doing enormous busi-
ness at Moore Park, and night has gained another
horror, for the larrikins of the neighbourhood
have picked up the Indian yells and war-cries. A
big boom in “ blood-curdlers ” is reported, and
Trapper Tom, or the Sioux Squaw of the Sus-
quehana,” is in great demand. Other tokens of
the prairie fever are also evinced in the assiduity
with which fowls and goats are lassooed by wild-
eyed lads armed with clothes-lines. One peculiar
thing about the show is the resemblance of the
Indians’ musical festival to a Salvation Army
gathering. The entertainment is one of the most
novel that Sydney has seen for years, but Dr.
Carver’s shooting is its most wonderful, if most
familiar, feature. The entrepreneur is showing
his level-headedness by preparing to take a “Wild
Australia” back to the States with him. The
Wirths have skipped to Brisbane.

The two great swordsmen, Parker and
Donovan, meet next Saturday at Coogee Aquarium
for the mounted swordsman championship of the
world and a purse. Captain M'Murdo will
referee the battle, and should it not be carried on
to his satisfaction, the prize will be withheld.
Manager Larmour informs us that they will meet
on foot the Saturday following, when Seguy is
expected also to chip in.

** * *
“Skipped by the Light of the Moon,” as
revived at Melbourne Opera House on Saturday
night, is a selection from the old familiar aggre-
gation of lunacies, redecorated by a company
well fitted to paint the turnip and gild unrefined
folly. Written for the Cirards, whose dramatic
talents soared about as high as they could kick,
“Skipped” formerly based its chief claim to
intellectual favour upon a wild chase round an
oyster-stall, and the measure of its success was
regulated by the ease and rapidity with which
the leading characters fell out of windows, or
toppled off a shaky garden wall. John Gourlay
introduced the thing to Australia in a slightly
improved form, and now, in conjunction with
Snazelle, he has worked up an entertainment
that has strong pretensions to the title of musical
comedy. Many of the new items, particularly
Snazelle’s drinking song and his rendering of the
Mephistopheles serenade from “Faust,” are ex-
cellent in themselves, and everything not new is
better presented than of yore—Gourlay’s own
grotesque performance not excepted. John
Forde, Henry Hudson, Aggie Kelton and Bessie
Vivian all participate freely in this wild, un-
trammelled joke, while Florence Fsdaile and
Lena Salenger came out stronger in the musical
than in the comedy direction. “ Skipped ” was
well received on Saturday, and with half a dozen
members of the late Nellie Stewart company
figuring on the bill it is bound to run awhile.
** * *
The Princess’s “Ida” is only out for
six nights, and the wisdom in withdrawing
“lolanthe ” to make room for this least tuneful of
the G. and S. operas was not made glaringly
apparent on Saturday. At no time a very power-
ful “ draw,” the show is hardly so attractive now
as of yore, but considering that Elton, Vernon,
and Leumane play their original characters, and
that Clara _ Merivale speaks the name-part
better than either Miss Colborne-Baber or Nellie
Stewart used to, there is little to find fault with in
thecast. Riley, as the baritone intruder upon
maiden retirement is more than usually suggestive
of poor Federici, who made his Australian debut
in the same capacity. Federici, however, used
to hurl his fascinations at the house in general,
whereas the more perfunctory Riley has a trick
of rolling his eyes at the private boxes, and per-
mitting his attention to wander listlessly round
the dress-circle. For the rest Miss Elsie Cameron
fails to develope any fresh meaning from the dull
song concerning the Mighty Must and Inevitable
Shall, and the heartiest laugh of the evening falls
to those three warriors who shed their glittering
accoutrements on the assumption that a man
must be an ass to fight in a cuirass. On Satur-
day (14th) a first revival of “The Yoeman of the
Guard ” is likely, for sundry reasons, to be better
received than was the first production of that
grand but gaudy work.
** * *
The Alexandra also promises an interest-
ing event for St. Valentine’s L>ay, as Dampier
makes a point of naming the anniversary of a for-
gotten festival. On this occasion Cupid’s dis-
credited agent is expected to incline the hearts of
young men and maidens towards “ The Miner’s
Right,” a drama which has been a long time pre-
paring for a fell swoop upon the sympathies of all
who believe that Australian literature is worthy
to he a profit in its own country. Meanwhile,
“The Black Flag” is dying hard to the accom-
paniment of a shrill gallery whistle.
** * *
“Doctor Bill,” at the Bijou, seems to go
with increasing velocity, and the spectacle of a
strong man in hysterics being carried bodily away
to the nearest pump is quite common in front.
For hilarious individuals like this a second dose
of “ Doctor Bill" is earnestly recommended. The
hollowness of all human mirth is soon apparent
to any man who comes up smiling for another look
at the acrobatic comedy, for after sitting it out to
the bitter end he rushes home to ask himself what
he meant by enjoying nothing at all the first time.
Yet “Doctor Bill ” is a success everywhere, a
fact which proves that a thin veneer of fun cleverly
placed on the commonest dramatic wood will sell
as well, or better, than the fine, ingrained humour
of articles like “Dandy Dick.” Anson and one
or two of the others are now clowning their parts,
and the roar of the groundlings at each fresh
variety of kick is something to be heard—and
pondered over.

expected, but Madame Cora, at St. George’s,
ought to trot her little show around the bush and
try her mesmeric powers upon the rabbits without
delay. By the time Madame has chosen her com-
mittee of investigation there is precious little
audience left to behold how she can fascinate the
grinning larrikin (probably at a “bob” a night)
with her glittering eye,

The Williamson and Garner lease of Mel-
bourne Royal expires in June, when, as now seems
almost certain, business will be carried on by a
Limited Company, consisting solely, or mainly, of
shareholders in the property, with George Coppin
and Dick Stewart as managing directors. The
veterans have already unfurled their banner, in
the form of a prospectus, and the necessary
capital is rolling up to support a venture which
will smack of the “good old days” with the per-
feshun in particular and the public in general. A
provisional tender for redecorating the house at a
cost of £2OOO has already been accepted, the very
best of scene-painting talent is willin’, and the old
Royal has a notion of resuming its proper social
position in about four months time. As Rignold
might observe —Once more into the breach,, dear
friends—old Harry ! Coppin ! and by George I
