Of all the days I loves the one
’Twixt Saturday and Mondi,
For that’s the day I meets my tart,
And takes her down to Bondi.
Let other coves crack up Coogee,
Or cribs of which they’re fond, I
Cannot forget, my oath ! could ye ?
The times I’ve ’ad at Bondi.
While I, when asked to take a drink,
The voice ’ave to respond, I
Shall alius down it with a wink,
And say—’Ere’s luck to Bondi.
At Poverty Point.
Holland, the showman, in his new Reminiscences, has
a funny bit about his apprenticeship to the drapery.
‘ Selling Off” and “Alarming Sacrifice” having failed
Mr. Giltgore decided to bring off a wreck, the first thinf
being to whitewash the windows, leaving only a few
finger-streaks tor the women to peep through, whereupon
a crowd gathered continually. Cheap goods were bought
and strewn over with seaweed in a most appetising way
while a diver walked up and down the shop in his cos-
tume, lecturing, “ Yes, mum, well do I remember that
’ere bit of calico. I was just a-draggin’ of it out when up
comes a monstrous shark. I outs with my knife,” &c.
“Dramaticus” writes that while J. C. M. Bellew had
much to do with Fechter’s Hamlet, the inspiration more
likely came, in the first instance, from Charley Dillon.
The latter’s Belphegor was a revelation, amid the tradi-
tional work of Phelps, Ohas. Kean, Vandenhoff, Creswick,
and Anderson, and then his purely natural Othello hit
hard, anticipating Fechter’s busines's in that part. Dillon
was the man who proved that the London public wanted
natural acting, wherefore Brooke did not go down, on his
return from Australia.
We got hold of a book the other day professing to
explain Robert Heller’s and Haidee Heller’s Second
Sight, and it started us sighting after Bob in the Empy-

Newnes got up a howling advertisement for his rag,
Titbits, at Christmas. He advertised for seven bald-
headed men, to sit in a row in the stalls, Drurv Lane
Theatre, Boxing Night, their heads lettered as follows:
T-i-t-b-i-t-8, but Gus Harris got madness, and swore it
shouldn’t be. The baldies were to be provided with free
tickets, and presented with dress-suits, also half-a-guinea
a nob. Didn’t come off—but the “ ad.” did.
A most impudent lecture was given in Manchester, the
other night, by Showman Holland, the waxy-moustached
Napoleonic caterer of the Surrey Alhambra, and good-
ness knows where, who fairly bangs Barnum on his own
brag. His Baby-Show attracted 2700 sucklings, and for
a boss baby he dressed up a dwarf, 35 years old, but only
feet high, over whom the women slobbered, at a res-
pectful distance, as he lay muling and puking in the
nurse’s arms, but some women found dwarfy, in his baby
clothes, smoking a pipe, and drinking a pewter of beer,
behind the scenes. Another of Holland’s dodges was
renting the vast Agricultural Hall, and opening it as
Holland’s Hippodrome, when he took over £3OOO the
opening day, a Boxing Day, although he entered on the
lesseeship without any horses or circus-men, trusting to
the luck of picking up what stray ones were about. Then he
dropped £7OOO in one week over some bulls which w ouldn’t
fight, though he says he gave £BOO each for them, and
had to sell them for £4 for butcher’s meat.

In the Greenwood Cemetery, New York, one of the
most beautiful burying-plaees in the world, there is a
neglected grave, grown over with a tangle of w-eeds and
tall rank grasses. On the headstone is the inscription,
“Sacred to the memory of Eliza Gilbert.” Few people
are aw'are that beneath lie the remains of the once famous
Lola Montez.

torical character, an actor named La Brassiere. Affiliated
with the Committee of Public Safety he saved the lives of
indiscreet Thespian comrades, amid them M. Fleury and

Revolutionist, was a retired actor, and it is said his guil-

been hissed there.
“Iconoclast” writes:—“l’m getting just sick of the
persistent repetition of the history of that really able
Australian actress, Miss Admirable Gingall. The fact of
her appearing in a pantomime very many years ago
cannot be got over, and so it is put as 16 years instead of
18 years. Many remember the occasion, with Miss
Admirable (then an infant), the biggest young lady in the
whole crowd, disporting a very fine bracelet. A philo-
sopher says the admixture of a lie doth ever give pleasure,
but please don’t keep rubbing it in—these things are to
be done with a feathery touch.”
Mrs. Brown-Potter’s mascotte is a fragment of the rope
with which a man was hanged. An old French supersti-
tion asserts that this pleasing little memento always
brings good luck, and consequently the Potter keeps her
specimen sewn in her corset, and the world goes well with
her in consequence. It occurs to us that the male
Brown-Potter wants about 18 feet of rope to bring him
any good fortune worth mentioning.
The friends of W. H. Williams, an old identity in the
Melbourne printing and publishing business, and one of
the fathers of the Metropolitan Liedertafel, are endeavour-
ing to arrange a testimonial and benefit concert for that
weary fragment. The alleged connection of Mr. Williams
with “the establishment of a Victorian literature” is
open to question, but his association with music down
South has been long, strong and altogether worthy of
recognition, more especially as he was always too ready
to let the musician stand in the printer’s light. Con-
sidering, however, that the veteran is supposed to have
special claims upon the Masons, it is remarkable that
they should sanction the present appeal to the general
public, and there seems little use in a man’s devotion to
a mystic brotherhood which allows him to feel the grip of
impecuniosity in his declining years.

Speaking of Madame Cora, the mesmerist, the Age
wisely observes: “None of the hypnotised individuals
could have been confederates, as they would not have
appeared so vacant-minded or have fallen about im-
promptu with such abandon,” This seems eminently
probable, when you look at it. Also the bank-embezzler
who disappeared lately couldn’t have been the real
criminal for he himself acknowledged that he didn’t take
the money, and besides he frankly admitted that he was
dead, so that it was no good looking after him. And
even the Premier Permanent directors reluctantly
allowed the fact to leak out that none of them were re-
sponsible, and that they were all as innocent as a new-laid
egg, and much as they wanted to go to gaol they felt that
they couldn’t conscientiously take up space 'that they
weren’t entitled to.

Charles Hawtrey has a habit of lolling round looking
vacantly at nothing in particular. Thus engaged one
evening, a haughty stranger approaching mistook him for
an attendant, and, presenting his ticket, said rather
imperiously, “My stall is No. 23.” Hawtrey, awakening
from his reverie, answered with a bland smile, “ I am
awfully glad to hear it, and now tell me how is your
wife ?”
Johnny Sheridan, who went to Maoriland to fill the
Potter-Bellew dates, is doing such big biz. (beating the
Opera, Warner, and Toole records) that he extends the
season everywhere. He travels with a big company, 32
in number, and a full ballet. That’s the secret! He is
playing Napier now, and commences a three weeks’
season in Auckland on February 16.
At a New York theatre there was an old scene-shifter
named Jackson, who, during one of Jefferson’s absences
died. On Jefferson’s return he asked the master car-
penter where Jackson was. The carpenter, with a sor-
rowful look, pointed upwards and said, quietly, “I guess
he’s shifting clouds.”
The N.Y. Actors’ Association has adopted the following
eminently sensible rule“ It shall not be allowable fin-
ally member while in the club-house to talk more than 40
consecutive minutes about himself or his histrionic
triumphs and experiences, unless it shall so happen that
there is no other member present who wishes to talk about
himself and his histrionic triumphs and experiences.”
Sir Arthur Sullivan’s grand opera, “ Tvanhoe,” at
D’Oyly Carte’s new theatre, is a great success. It has a
double cast of the leading parts, because the work is too
hard for the same people every night, and is a heavy pro-
duction of the Verdi or Meyerbeer class.

The long and curious intimacy between Lily Langtry
and Fred. Gebhardt seems to have been interrupted for
ever. The relations were strained at the time Langtry
last left America, but when Gebhardt heard that the fair
creature was ill in London he started at once across the
ocean, determined to effect a reconciliation. As soon as
he got to London he hastened to call on Langtry. As
luck would have it, he arrived at her hotel at the very
tune when the Prince of Wales was paying his respects to
the lady. His card was returned with the message that
he would have to wait awhile—that Mrs. Langtry could
receive no other visitor while Turn was calling. Words
would fail to convey even an idea of the intensity of Mr.
Gebhardt’s wrath; he flounced out of the hotel, went
hack to his own rooms, packed his luggage, and started
for Liverpool by the first train. With this touching inci-
dent ended the flirtation which for years has been a
notorious and flagrant scandal. Gebhardt has betaken
himself to the sports of the turf, and Langtry is losing
her money in London trying to make people believe she
can act.

YOUTH AND AGE.
When all the world is young, lad, and all the trees are
green,
And every goose a swan, lad, and every lass a queen,
Then, hey for boot and horse, lad, and ride the world
away;
Young blood must have its course, lad, and every dog his
day.
When all the world is old, lad, and all the trees are
brown,
And all the sport is stale, lad, and all the wheels run
down;
Creep home and take thy place there, thy early friends
among ;
God grant you find one face there you lov'd when all was
young.
The Queen’s Uniform.
I went into a public-house to get a pint of beer.
The publican ’e up and sez “ We serve no redcoats
here.”
The girls behind the bar they laughed, and giggled
fit to die;
I outs into the street again, an’ to myself sez I,
Oh, it’s Tommy this, an’ Tommy that, an’ Tommy
go away,
But it’s “Thank you, Mister Atkins,” when the
Band begins to play.
1 went into a theatre, as sober as could be,
They gave a drunk civilian room, but ’adn’t none
for me;
They send me to the gallery, or round the music
'alls,
But when it comes to fighting, Lord ! they’ll shove
me in the stalls,
For it’s Tommy this, an’Tommy that, an’ “Tommy,
wait outside,”
But it’s “special train for Atkins” when the
trooper’s on the tide.
Oh, makin’ mock o’ uniforms that guard you while
you sleep
Is cheaper than them uniforms, an’ they’re star-
vation cheap;
An’ hustling drunken sodgers when they’re going
large a bit
Is ten times better business than paradin’ in full
kit.
Then it’s Tommy this, an’ Tommy that, an’
“Tommy’ow’s your soul?”
But it’s “ thin red line of ’eroes ” when the drums
begin to roll.
Wo aren’t no thin red ’eroes, nor we aren't no
blackguards too,
But single men in barracks, most remarkably like
you.
And, if sometimes our conduck isn't all your fancy
paints,
Why, single men in barracks don't grow into
plaster saints.
While it’s Tommy this, an’ Tommy that, an’
“Tommy, fall behind,”
But it’s “please to walk in front, si) 1 ,” when
there’s trouble in the wind.
You talk of better food for us, an’ schools, an
fires, an’ all;
Well wait for extra rations if you treat us
rational.
I)oirt mess about the cook-house slops, but prove
it to our face
The Widow’s Uniform is nut the soldierman’s
disgrace.
I or it’s Tommy this, an’ Tommy that, an’ “ chuck
him out, the brute,”
But it’s “Saviour of his Country ” when the guns
begin to shoot.

“The Great Pro-Consul."
SKETCHES FROM A LIFE OF ACTION.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
m.
Having seen the Constitution in full
operation, Grey applied to the Imperial Govern-
ment for temporary leaveof absence, andon the olst
December, 1853, he left New Zealand after eight
years of vigorous and able government, during
which time he had ruled Europeans and natives
with all but absolute power. .
He was, however, not long left to enjoy his
otinm cum dignitate in England. Troubles had
broken out at the Cape, with which the Governor
of that colony was quite unable to cope, and Sir
George Grey’s remarkable success in dealing with
the Maoris pointed to him as a fitting person to
overcome the South African difficulty.
The situation was peculiar. The great Jvafhr
chief, Krielie, determined to destroy foreign
dominion, had schemed for the initiation of a
war of extermination against the colonists. His
plan was to strip his people of all their cattle, to
destroy all their corn, and prohibit cultivation in
every form, and then, when they were all driven
mad with hunger, to launch them on the settle-
ments in a struggle for existence. As a pre-
liminary he had secured the co-operation of all the
rain-doctors; and a prophet of renown took up
the parable, which was re-echoed by all the rest,
that cultivation must forthwith cease, har-
vested crops be destroyed, the land de-
vastated, and all the cattle killed. This
would be followed, it was prophesied, by
a resurrection of all the cattle, together
with the cattle of their ancestors, all greatly im-
proved in size and quality and countless in
numbers. All who obeyed and followed the com-
mand of the prophet by killing their cattle, should
feed tor the rest of their lives on unlimited beef;
but the disobedient would be turned into moths.
These mandates from Heaven were obeyed to
the letter; the cattle were slain, the whole
country denuded and desolated and the colonists
thrown into the wildest state of alarm. Sir George
Grey was, in 1855, hastily appointed Governor of
the Cape, and despatched from England specially
to meet the emergency. As with the Maoris, he
first marshalled his forces, saw that his powder
was dry, and then turned his attention to settling
the difficulty once and for all with the least
possible bloodshed. Having secured communica-
tions and defended points most open to attack, the
Governor next proceeded to deal more immediately
with Krielie's tribe. He there found the Kaffirs
starving by thousands, and in no state to pour
into the settlements as devastating warriors.
Instead of fighting these wretched victims of
superstition. Sir George wisely fed them. He
sent them copious supplies, and was soon able to
dictate terms of peace to the haughty old chief
without firing a shot. By treaty lie obtained
from Krielie all the fine country lying between tin-
Great Kie River and the River Guabakka, known
as Fingo-land.
# ♦ *
One of the greatest services rendered to
the natives by Grey w r as the establishment and
partial endowment of Loved ale. A native school
was about to be established there under Presby-
terian auspices. The Governor called on the pro-
moters and pointed out to them how very little
influence for good, beyond the immediate welfare
of the actual inmates of the school, would attend
mere book instruction, whether religious or
secular. On the other hand he contended that by
the establishment of workshops and the appoint-
ment of instructors in various branches of manu-
facturing industries, the good effected would be
distributed through the whole community. He
expressed his willingness, if his views were
adopted, to pay the main coat of the buildings
and workshops, and to guarantee the payment of
£2)00 per annum during the term of his Governor-
ship. The school w r as built and endowed accord-
ingly’— and run on purely undenominational lines,
while technical instruction in all branches was
imparted to young Kaffirs.
In 1880 a book was issued from this establish-
ment giving the names and the after fortunes of
10,000 Kaffirs who, having been instructed in
different branches of trade at had been
distributed all over South Africa in the exercise of
their divers trades. Sir George Grey, to whom a
copy of the book was sent w ith a suitable letter,
declared that the jicrusal of it had afforded him
more pleasure than any event of the last few years
that he could call to mind.
At the same time he also endowed an Industrial
School for natives at Healdtown under Wesleyan
management. The Government paid £7OOO for
building purposes and endowed it with £lOOO per
annum. . ,
When Grey left the Capo again to assume the
Governorship of New Zealand, in 1861, his suc-
cessor partially withdrew’ this Government appro-
priation. Healdtown, under money-grubbing
“religious ” management —the mercenary mission
system —at once collapsed. Lovedale is still car-
ried on and continues to flourish, having an
average attendance of 400 Kaffir scholars.
• • •
Towards the end of the Crimean War the
English Government raised a large force of Ger-
mans for military service, and, on the war sud-
denly ceasing soon after the enrolment of these
men, agreed to give such as desired it, in lieu of
. other compensation or continuance of pay, free
passages to the Cape and grants of land in that
colony. Large numbers accepted the offer, but, on
their arrival at the Cape, Grey declined to place
them on the land, except under certain conditions.
lie explained that the men constituted the very
I Hist class of immigrants, provided they were
afforded opportunities for banning family men ;
that there were no such opportunities .in the
colony, where the supply of marriageable women
was already quite inadequate to the demands, or
rather requirements, of the population, and that
lie saw nothing but incentives to vice and degrada-
tion in sending a large body of men, all in the
prime and vigour of life, away from their homes,
into a strange land, and without any possibility ol
forming family ties. He, therefore, made it a
condition of settlement that an agency should be
established for sending out the female friends and
relations of the new immigrants to join them in
the colony, under proper regulations ; and as the
men had meanwhile arrived, he sent instructions
and lists of these females, furnished by the Ger-
mans, to a Hamburg agent.
The Colonial office took serious umbrage at this,
and an angry correspondence ensued, which ended
in him being suddenly recalled to England,
nominally for another reason, but Sir George Grey
declares actually on account of this German immi-
gration business. Immediately on his arrival in
London he was met by the Hamburg agent, who
told him that the Government had refused to meet
his advances, and that unless £20,000 were placed
at his disposal the whole scheme would fall
through. Sir George, then and there, gave a
cheque for the amount on his private account,
telling the agent to take promissory-notes at long
dates from the different persons to whom the
advances were made. It is gratifying to learn
that this money was all repaid by the German
emigrants, to the last farthing.
But the most amusing part of the transaction
was that during Sir George’s voyage to England a
change of Ministry had taken place, and those in
office, when he arrived, fully approved of his
action, reversed the decision of their predecessors,
and at once reinstated him as Governor.
During .Sir Henry Loch's tour as Governor
through British Kaffraria in the early part of last
year one great feature was a procession of German
settlers in costumes illustrating the industries,
crafts, trades, &c., pursued by them. Then
principal sjiokesinan informed the Governor that
they represented the settlement which had been
founded by Grey, and that through his wise pre-
cautions they were now the most thriving body ot
settlers in British Kaffraria.
In this matter of land settlement, he states that
his policy at tin* Cape was to afford all facilities to
Civil servants of every capacity to settle down on
land with their families at the termination of
their time of service. In 1878, when he was head
of the New Zealand Ministry, Sir George pre-
pared a measure with the object of enabling Civil
The modern method of inculoatin good behaviour as taught (at Trin. Col., Melb.) by
the Canons of the Church.
servants to select land with the view of eventually
occupving it, but the defeat of Ins Ministry ana
his consequent retirement from office prevented it
from coming forward.
Sir George, therefore, proposed that any civil
servant might Is. authorised to make a selection
of land under certain conditions, and might coni-
mence improvement# on it and carry them out
even while still in the service, so preparing a
place for retirement on expiration of service.
But the selector would acquire no title to such
land until a certain number of years of occupation
intervened after his leaving the service. As a
matter of course misconduct in the service would
cause cancellation of the selection. This system
would completely do away with all claims for pen-
sions, retiring allowances, Ac.
• • •
Soon after Sir George Grey's return to the
Capo he placed himself in a position that enabled
him to perform, perhaps, the greatest service that
ever was performed by the Governor of a depen-
dency. Not the least extraordinary part of the
story is the manner in which this magnificent ser-
vice was recognised—a recognition j Kissing over
the head of the performer and bestowed on the
two persons who of all others had least to do
"(hi 29th Feb., 185fi, Lord Canning assumed office
as Governor General of India, nrc the Marquis of
Dalhousie, and soon after the gravest symptoms
of disaffection became manifest among the Sepoy
troops of the army of Hindostan. Sir George
Grey, alarmed at the indications of a general
mutiny, wrote to the Governor-General on the
subject, but the latter attached no signification to
the symptoms. On 10th May. 1857, the mutiny
broke out at Meerut, and Sir George again wrote
urging the immediate strengthening of the Euro-
pean forces at the command of the Government,
and offering to send every available maif, but
Isud Canning could not read the writing on the
wall, and declaml that with a few hundred
additional cavalry he would easily knock the
mutiny on the head. , , , .
Meanwhile, England had embarked on her
second opium war with China, and Lord Elgin
was despatched as Her Majesty’s Ambassador
Extraordinary to the Emin-ror. Ho arrived o t
Hong Kong on 2nd July, 1857. Ships of war,
gunboats* and transport-ships with several regi-
ments of troops were sent en route to China, to
back up Elgin. _ -rr
On their way they touched at the Cape. 1
Sir George Grey was now restored to the position
of Governor, and though the Governor-General ol
India was blind, the Governor of the Cape was
fully alive to the exigencies of the situation.
Fortunately, also, the officer in command of the
troops in the first transport ship which arrived in
Cape Town, Brigadier Adrian Hope (vvno shortly
afterwards fell desperately wounded at the Siege
of Lucknow) was his personal friend.
The Governor had a conference with Adrian
Hope, told him that in his opinion the mutiny had
assumed such proportions that it rendered the
employment of every European who could near
arms a matter of absolute necessity, which nan
not been foreseen when troops were being des-
patched to China, and that there was but a scanty
provision of European troops in India. He con-
fessed that though as Governor of the Cape, and
therefore Commander-in-Chief of the lorces in
that territory he had power to command move-
ments of troops within the district, he had no
power to direct movements outside of Ins com-
mand, and he put it to his friend whether, with
the later intelligence which he had received, if he
did not think that it would be for the good of the
country to divert his course from China to India.
Brigadier Adrian Hope recognised the urgency
of the situation and Sir George Grey, having
succeeded in diverting the first ship, there was
no difficulty with the remainder. the whole
China army was directed on India.
Meanwhile Lord Elgin, at Hong Kong, was
vainly awaiting the arrival of an army to back up
his demands. On the 16th July, 1857, hearing no
news of its coining, he set sail for Calcutta, where
he learned that his forces were on the march to
Delhi. The noble lord was furious. Sir George
Grey had at once written explaining the action he
had taken, and the reasons which had prompted
him ; but Lord Elgin was too indignant to vouch-
safe any reply to his letter, and made his way
back to Hong Kong, which he reached in Sep-
teinber.
Meanwhile, the great service rendered by Sir
George Grey had been promptly acknowledged in
other quarters. Lord Elphiustone, Governor of
Bombay, had declared in Council that mu-Ii prompt
action had saved India and preserved it to tlm
British Crown. The Under Secretary of the Indian
Department, Mr. Henry Laboucliere, had, by
instruction, written to Sir George, and informed
him that Her Majesty's acknowledgment of his
forethought would shortly follow in official form.
And then—Sir George Grey never heard anything
more about it.
All the papers in sup|>ort of the alvive state-
ment are now to be seen in the Manusoiipt
department of the Auckland Public Library.
Why were Sir George’s services ignored so com-
pletely, after having been so fully acknowledged I 1
In iHT>S a gentleman of the highest standing at
the Cap.- and with great political influence in
England happened to ask this question of a
Cabinet Minister in the course of an after-dinner
conversation. The answer was equally informal,
but ran as follows :
For political reasons it was necessary to retain
the services of the Governor-General. Lord Can-
ning, as Viceroy on the abolition of the East India
Company's Charter. Now, by recognising that
Sir George Grey had been more far-seeing than
Lord Canning— that the former had actually
despatched troops in despite of the latter had
Kit down a mutiny which the latter had not
ilieved in—Lord Canning’s claim to Is- con-
tinued as ruler of the Indian Empire would have
been minimised. Therefore, Sir George Greys
undoubted claims to consideration would have to
remain in abeyance for the present, but would
receive full consideration ere long.
Ere long Lord Canning died, and lo! and
behold ! Lord Elgin was selected to succeed him
as Viceroy of India. In a very short time Lord
Elgin also died. And then it became essential
that all idea of recognising Sir George Grey’s
great services in putting down the Indian Mutiny
should disapjiear from the ken of the English
Government, for it was deemed necessary to award
a large pension to the widow of the deceased earl,
and this pension wan awarded in consideration of
the distinguished services of her deceased husband
in hurryingon troops from the Cape for the sup-
pression of the Indian Mutiny !!!
Thus the Governor-General who did not believe
In the mutiny got splendid rewards Is-canse Grey
did believe in it; and the Commander-in-Chicf
who resented the despatch of Ids troops for sup-
pressing' this mutiny got a splendid reward
(through his widow) because Grey despatched
them !

Stanley had just returned from his raid
through Central Africa, and had published a por-
tion of his so-called discoveries, and Lord Salis-
bury had issued his bullying ultimatum to poor
little Portugal, when Sir George Grey said in dis-
cussing the subject
“ I suppose you remember Rumford and his
discoveries? Towards the •nd of the last century,
Rumford, an American in the service of the
Bavarian Government, announced his great dis-
covery that heat consists in motion. He was
invited to Berlin ; there was great excitement,
cannon were placed at his disposal, and horses for
supplying motive power, with the result that by
friction in boring the cannon so much heat was
generated that water surrounding the cannon was
brought up to boiling point. Well, Rumford made
a good thing of it; from Mr. he became Count;
he was awarded a large pension, became the
observed of all observers, and a leading scientist
of his day.
“ At the very same time an aboriginal of New
South Wales came in from the bush, threw down
a bundle of opossums to his gin, who pro-
ceeded to rub two pieces of wood together
until they burst into flame, and then cooked
her lord's dinner, which he squatted down and
ate, throwing to her scraps of it over his shoulder.
You see, the Australian black knew that heat was
an outcome of motion, and he and his ancestors
had practised it for generations, but ho got no
countship, no pensi n, no kudos for putting the
natural law in act'on —lie only got his dinner.
“So with Stanley and his discoveries. He is
only taking credit for what was discovered long
before he came into existence, and England is
taking advantage of his second-hand discoveries.
Proof of this may be seen in the Auckland Public
Library.” _ ,
Proceeding accordingly to the Library, Sir
George took down a small octavo volume in very
old calf and said ; “Here is a little book that 1
picked up as a bargain, for its value then was not
known. There are only three copies extant with
the two maps included, this is one of them, and
yet 1 got it for £35.
“Its very title will show you the wonderful
nature of its contents as illustrating the value of
modern African discovery.”
The following is the full title of the book m
question:—
A RKI’ORT OK THE KIKODOME OF CONGO, A REGION OF
Africa.—And of the Countries that border rounde
ABOUT THE SAME.
1. Wherein is also shewed that the two zones 2 ornda
and Frigida are not only habitable, but inhabited, and
very temperate, contrary to the opinions of the old
philosophers.
■2. That the blaoke colour which is in the skinnes of the
Ethiopians and Negroes, &c., proceeded! not from the
snnne.
That the River Nilvs springeth not out of the moun-
tains of the Moone, as hath heene heretofore beleeuved :
Together with the true cause of the rising and increasing
thereof.
4. Besides the description of divers plants, Fishes and
Iteastes that arc found in those countries.
Drawenoutof the writings and discourses of Odoardo
I.epez, a Portingall, by Philipps Pigafetta.
Translated out of Italian by Abraham Hartwell.
London, 1588.
Turning then to the maps, Sir George pointed
out that the mountains of the moon were properly
placed- the Congo traced to its sources. The
lakes now known as Victoria, Nyauza, Albert
Nyanza, and Albert Edward Nyanza, all duly
located, with the Nile issuing from its due
sources; Tanganyika recorded ; and Stanley’s
so-called great discovery, the bend in the Congo,
correctly set down ; while the whole Shire district
was also mapped out.
“ And now,” said Sir George, “ we will turn to
other proofs afforded by a greater discoverer and
better man than Stanley.” He accordingly pro-
duced a file of twelve original letters from
Livingstone, written to him as Governor of the
Cape during the progress of the great traveller
through Central Africa. In one of these (none of
them have ever yet lieen published, though all
are open to inflection in the Auckland Library),
Livingstone speaks of his astonishment, while pro
ceeding through theUpperShini district,on finding
himself among tribes who professed Christianity,
and who, in their invocations to the Deity, used
forms of prayer but little altered from the words
which they had learned from their.TesuitPortuguese
instructors far more than 100 years ago. These sire
as nearly as possible Livingstone’s own words,
repeated from memory, in the absence of the
original. Livingstone gives some 20 prayers, &e.,
as taken down by him from dictation, including
the Pater, Credo. Ave. Maria, Litanies, Confiteor,
and many others.
The subject might be pursued further, but
enough has been said to discount the claims of
Stanley as an original discoverer.

West Australia’s public debt is £1,284,079,
or about £23 per head of population.
The insurrection in Portugal has failed.
Yet the Republic is not dead, but only sleepeth—
as in Spain.
Post-prandial Parkes has declared that
Treasurer MAlillan is “a strong-minded man.”
Does he mean postprandial MAlillan, or the
morning edition thereof?
“ Boys Wanted ’ Such is the familiar
legend outside the. labour-office door. What for?
Why, to take men’s places, and grow up into
cheap—because uneducated —labour.
Sir 'Envy says that his “ dear friend, Mr,
Brunker, has homespun abilities.” It is sooth-
ing to know that Brunker partially reciprocates
this cordiality, and frequently alludes to Parkes
as his “ very dear friend.”
Alexander Oliver, Registrar of Friendly
Societies and Parliamentary draughtsman of
N.S. Wales, emphatically told the Labour Com-
mission that every new trades-union that is formed
is an improvement of existing conditions.
Arabi, the Egyptian, has made a piteous
appeal for mercy, to the relentless Salisbury
( twas Gladstone who banished him eight years
ago.) There is no nation like England for putting
down the English spirit. Anybody but an
Anglican can be patriotic.
The Spanish General Election has resulted
conservatively. The Conservatives followed
Disraeli’s methods in Spain, and gave the masses
votes, knowing that they would sway for the
Monarchy and the Roman Camarilla. This will last
only for a time, till Education trickles and distils.
“TheN.Z. Government is almost wholly
composed of self-made men,” Then the labor-
party has little to hope from it. The Boimderby
of Australasia is generally a man of callous heart
and adamantine conscience. The self-making of
one usually necessitates the undoing of ten thou-
sand.
Parkes says that “sometimes monkeys”
have climbed to the top of the poll. Hearken to a
parable:—
“Free labourers!” Rounded up like
sheep by police hirelings, in what are they free?
Bowing down to the irresponsible tyranny of
banded capital, for permission to live, where is
their liberty ? Stealing the life rights of their
manlier brethren, and, for the offal bones cast to
them, embracing wage-slavery, it is the very
needle’s point of satire to dub them “free
labourers. ”

And give the lips we love unborrowed bread.’
To which we reply:—
Premier Parkes declares that the only
existing political question that need be fought out
as a party one is the coming struggle of Protection
r. Freetrade. The country should paste this in
its hat, and watch the Old Man’s future
manoeuvres with the One Man One Vote question,
labour legislation, and other cognate reforms that
have hitherto only had a bogus place in the plat-
form of the party gerrymandered by this cunning
old posturer.
Sir Henry Parkes says it is not inconsis-
tent with democratic principles to raise statues to
our public men. That may be so, but it is not con-
sistent with purely democratic ideas that a statue
to such a democrat as John Dunmore Lang should
be inaugurated by an English Earl, a G.C.M.G.,
a K.C.M.G., and the wealthiest house-and-land-
owner in Sydney. Unless Mr. George Thornton
or Mr. G. R. Dibbs can ho called democrats The
Bulletin fails to see where democracy was repre-
sented at the inauguration.
Several of the Sydney dailies are pleased
to attribute the failure of the recent meeting of
the N.S.W. railway employes, re the dismissal of
Mr. Hoyle, to the satisfaction felt in the service
at the event in question. No more disgusting
falsehood was ever penned. The N.S.W. em-
ployes are not satisfied, but they are too cowed
by the high-handed action of the Railway Com-
missioners and the non-interference of the Go-
vernment to take steps until the general election,
when the ballot will speak with a voice of thunder.
Aha! The Scotch courts have strangled
the trades-unions—have clapped an embargo on
£20,000 strike funds of the railway servants,
because of directors’ actions for breach of agree-
ment. Why wasn’t this tried in Australia?—
Now, why ? We should like to have seen it
tried—just gently, to see how wo could stand it.
But it will yet be tried. The Employers’ Union
will lay a hand like a whale’s ear on all the
strike funds, but the associated workers won't bo
able to lay hands on the funds of tiio Employers’
Union.

Labor organises in Victoria. The Trades
Hall is preparing a gigantic octopean ramification
for next general election, and will swamp Parlia-
ment with Labor candidates. If Graham Berry
had to begin over again, he would get a magni-
ficent show —the berries will grow on every bush.
Tin; other day Berry, now Sir Graham, wrote out
from London asking whether if ho returned to
Victoria he could get the leadership of the
Liberals. “ No,” replied his friend, “ your name
is now never mentioned here.” False ! We heard

Judas Iscariot.
Just think again over Robespierre's
vitality—his name keeps shooting over the ocean
a century after that horrible day when Sanson
thrust him under the guillotine, in Thcrmidor.
The explanation is in Mirabcau’s remark “That
young man will go far. He believes what he
says.” There were fifty men of more ability than
Robespierre in the French Re\olution, but they
lacked faith. Men like Bright and Glad tone
think they can go a certain length and then pull
the people up, l>y their own overwhelming person-
ality. Vain ! Miraboau thought so, but Marie
Antoinette bought him.

Gumboil, will soon come into the five million
pounds sterling left him by his late uncle the
brother of the first Earl Cairns-and hopes to be
returned as M.P. for Southampton at next elec-

A new poet, John Ernest M‘Cann, has
perpetrated tho shortest and most suggestive
pomelet on record:
“To You.
Sweetest and best,
God knows the rest.”
Premier Griffith, of Queensland, while in
Melbourne, was the guest of Premier Munro.
Griffith, the hard-headed, is credited with a
potentiality, under dire pressure, of a-bottle-of-
whisky-a-day. Munro is a never-touch-it-nor-
keep-it-for-my-friends. How did they compro-
mise ?
Gill, a strapping Irishman, is the coming,
or rather arrived, London barrister. In the recent
case of Marks v. Butterfield, he quite lorded it
over Sir Charles Russell, for a whole week, and
Geo. Lewis, theatrical attorney, was so delighted
that he made Gill free of his house. Lewis has
a raw on Russell.
A man named Steers, on the Queensland
border, went to a finance firm six months ago and
obtained a loan, giving as security a mortgage on
five white steers. It did not strike the financier
that anything was wrong until, the other day, he
levied on the security. Then he found that the
steers were the sons of the old man.
When G overnor Jersey was a wild college
youth he was “ accommodated ” by the Hebrews
to an alarming extent. On coming of age and
.succeeding to the big pile which an accident of
birth had filched for him, he was advised to take
no notice of the “debts of his minority.” But
Jersey was not built that way. He paid all, with
5 per cent, added.
Newberry, the Adelaide defaulting official
(formerly a gubernatorial attache), is supposed to
have struck for California—which it is the local
fashion to do. Woods and Dnnsterville, both
formerly of the S.A. Civil service, will probably
welcome the latest arrival. In each case the
absconder could have been easily caught had
certain influential persons wished.

refuses to accept the olive-branch from Dr.
Maloney, M.P., ere the little Doctor goes to
Europe. Tom don’t mind so much for himself,
but there is his depaited and maligned parient,
one of the gentlest creatures that ever breathed :
so ho indites a flaming letter to the papers, after
firing off his horse-pistol, and sniffing the smoke—
Aha-a !
-Lieutenant-Colonel G. R. Browne, last
week sentenced in Sydney to three months’ gaol
for false pretences (he had bought clothes with a
valueless cheque for £J, out of which he received
£ll2s. (id. change), has been convicted perhaps a
dozen times, and is on the subject of worthless
cheques an absolute lunatic. He is a distinguished
army-officer, having been present at Alma, Inker-
man, and at the assault on the Malakoff, where
he lost an arm. Afterwards he served in India,
and was present at the Relief of Lucknow. Issuing
bad cheques appears to be his only vice.
While Sir John Robertson was on an
electioneering tour, some 20 years ago, he encoun-
tered a young mother, who held up her offspring
to be kissed by the candidate, after the fashion of
the Hon. S. Slumkey. Sir John did his duty
without any outward or visible sign of repug-
nance ; and the father, interposing, informed him
that the baby was named Algernon. “ Alger-
non !” demanded tho knight; “do you want to
damn the child for ever? Call him Tom, Dick,
or Harry, and make an honest man of him ; call
him Algernon and you’ll make him into a billiard-
marker !”

Society
A certain great statesman took unto him-
self a second wife, who was as young as the
daughters he had begotten by his first. The
second Mrs. Statesman, immediately on her
enthronement, impressively informed her new
daughters-in-law that she was “going to be mis-
tress in this establishment.” Whereupon, one of
the girls sweetly remarked; “It will come easy
t) you to b* mistress inside the house; you've
been long enough a mistress outside of it.”
# * #
The charming widow whom the clergyman
of a certain fashionable Sydney church refused to
hitch the other day because the ceremony had
already been performed, was formerly engaged
to a naval officer. The story goes that so far had
matters advanced that their trousseau was actually
ordered from England, and, with a sentiment
which was charming in its simplicity and subtle in
its origin, the lady arranged to have the various
articles of dress packed jointly; his walking coats
with her walking dresses, his hats with her
bonnets, etc. But, alas ! by the time the ship came
to hand whispering tongues had poisoned truth,
the match was off, and the fair romanticist was re-
luctantly compelled to sort out the gallant captain’s
pyjamas for her robes dennit, and—well, and soon.
» # *
This reminds one in a roundabout
way that a certain other service mag-
nate was aforetime engaged to a wealthy
Australian lady. Sending her one day a
note full of delicious love bon-bons, deli-
cacies calculated to make a grandmother’s heart
flutter, while getting his English letters ready, he
snnehow inserted as ip intended for foreign con-
sumption only. It contained one reference to his
fin meet that though complimentary, was yet out-
spoken enough to break the match off: “I am
delighted to be able to assure you that I love
more, and her money less, daily.”
# # #
Valentines are nearly dead. Christmas
cards have killed them, and they go the way of
the pantomimes. The postman is delighted on
the whole. His load of bleeding hearts was more
than the butcher’s. But then he misses all the
girls’ sweet smiles, which he took unto himself.
One who was once well-known as a rising
man of business in Sydney, but who is now an
inmate of Liverpool Benevolent Asylum, attributes
his dismal failure in life to the corrupting in-
fluences of Old Jack Robertson. Thus : The pre-
sent recipient of charity was an ironmonger’s
assistant, when Old Jack was Premier. One
Saturday morning Jack entered the ironmonger’s
shop and ordered some wire netting, with—
“ Mind you, now, send the (blank) stuff down to-
day without fail, because I (crimson) well want to
(clotty) well build a(rufous) hen-house to-morrow.”
“ Oh, but,” timidly ventured the ironmonger’s
assistant, who had been piously reared but whose
reverence for goodness was struggling with his
fear of offending the most popular and powerful
man in N.S.W., “oh, but to-morrow is Sunday,
Sir John.” “Sunday or no (cherry) Sunday,”
gruffly replied Old Jack, “I mean to build that
(incarnadine) hen-house to-morrow.” It was the
first insidious crack in the young man’s conception
of the Fourth Commandment; he insensibly fell
away from grace ; the cleavage in his ideas of the
church catechism became wider ; he morally pined
away and is now spiritually dead, while his lines
are cast in the rough ruts of cold charity officially
dispensed. Doth Old Jack still Pharaoh his
heart ?
* * *
The baronet who was discovered cheating
Tummy and other distinguished persons at
cards, and who has (says the cable) fled to
the Continent, will, writes a correspondent,
probably turn out to be the man often re-
ferred to in The Bulletin as having taken lessons
from a skilled sharper in the parlour of a flash
pub. The Bulletin baronet is “a well known
figure in London society,” and his name begins
with J. His confederate is, or was, a nobleman
of higher rank, who is still alive and friendly with
the Prince of Wales. These two men always
came together to take their lessons in shuffling,
marking, dealing, and so forth, from the expert.
They made a half-and-half attempt to disguise
themselves for these visits to the pub., and their
exact identities remained a mystery until the
master one day spotted his two pupils in the Royal
Enclosure at Ascot, when a toney member of the
talent told him their names. If J, has fled to the
Continent, The Bulletin will improve the
occasion still further, and try to put a little one
in for the other fellow. The accused baronet’s
name, as per cable, turns out to be Gordon-
Cumming, a scion of a family of hitherto irre-
proachable reputation.

What to do with the rubbish of Sydney is
now a leading question. Many of the aldermen
say tow it out to sea. They now tow out to sea
the refuse from the slaughter-houses, and thus it
happens that, at intervals, the beach at Manly is
covered with putrescent entrails for hundreds of
yards, and that when you go in to bathe a pair of
bullock’s lungs suddenly arises on a breaker and
slaps you in the face.
# * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph, which chaffs
British editors on their lamentable ignorance of
Australian geography, placidly, refers to the
“John Darien” (!) station in connection with
shearing troubles. Now, it was at Jondaryan
station that Duke Alfred, of Edinburgh, was pic-
nicked on his first visit to the Darling
Downs. Arthur Hodgson (afterwards degraded
into Sir Arthur Hodgson) had a “damper”
made—a monster the size of a cartwheel—but
Alfred could neither be coaxed nor coerced into
tasting this specimen of bushman’s fare, and
giggled out, “I hope you’ll take this as eaten.”
Which was sufficient toestablishhis reputation as a
wit, the flunkeys around him declaring that it
wasthebestthingthey overheard. Nevertheless, the
D.T. has forgotten Jondaryan, the birthplace of the
wittiest thing ever said in Australia. Again, it
was but last year that Jondaryan was before all
Australia as the station at which began the suc-
cessful attempt of shearers to humble the Darling
Downs squatters.

Alison’s Cauonbar and Maryvale Stations,
beyond Nyngan, N.S.W., were sold a few days
ago to Mr. James Ranker, of Wyong, for
£192,721. They comprise 1,100,000 acres 1
# # •
The mention of these big figures recalls,
in some esoteric way, a story once related to us by
a wealthy squatter, and which is tellingly
illustrative of tiie startling ups and downs by no
means uncommon in pastoral life. “ Many years
ago,” said the old pioneer, “ 1 was sheep-super-
intendent on one of the biggest Riverine pro-
perties. There fell upon the country a terrible
drought, which endured for so long that pastoral
losses ran into millions of pounds. Our nearest
neighbours were the ’s, who had been many
years engaged in squatting, and had invested in
their Riverine station about £lOO,OOO, most of it
their own money. This long and terrible drought
ruined them. They found themselves hopelessly
indebted to the bank, and the bank came down
upon them —it had to. When the end came, the
miseries of the drought had utterly demoralised
the ’s. They left what had for many years
been their home, and when they arrived wearily at
the out-station at which I had my head-quarters,
they were literally without boots to their feet.
Mrs. , a highly - educated and tenderly-
nurtured lady, was in a pitiable plight, though
she bore up bravely. Her toes were sticking out
out of holes in her boots. The children were,
absolutely barefooted. The whole party were
moneyless and actually hungry. I vacated my
hut for the benefit of the mother and bairns, and
slept under a gumtree. They continued their
journey only to encounter fresh troubles. They
were suddenly bailed up by flood-waters, and
marooned for 48 hours on an island, without food
and with the odds in favour of their being
drowned. Now, had they been allowed to retain
possession of their station, the very floods
that threatened to engulf them would have made
their fortune, for the A ’s, who bought the
station, cleared £200,000 out of it in a very little
while. It never seemed to occur to the A ’s,
however, to send a cheque to their unfortunate
predecessors. Mrs. afterwards had to take
in washing in order to earn a living. Some friends
to whom she applied for laundry-work, tried to
persuade her to give up rough work for teaching,
but Mrs. grimly replied : “It is far better
for a lady to be a washerwoman than a governess! ”
# # *
We are informed in the scientific column
of a Brisbane paper that the reflected heat of the
moon is just equal to that of a lighted candle at a
distance of 21ft. Anybody who has ever been
paid his wages by the reflection of Parkes’ pro-
missory-note, 21ft. away, will understand the
situation.
* # *
The fab English flunkey is beginning to
make himself apparent in Australia. He may bo
found now on any summer afternoon loafing beside
his owner’s carriage in George-street, Sydney,
with his degraded head disguised in powder or
flour, and a miserable coloured coat and top-boots
piled on top of his carcase as a public announce-
ment that he is kept to do, for another man, any
kind of household job which is so mean that the
other man won't do it himself. He is generally
young and sturdy, and about as good-looking as
an alleged human being can be who is always
grovelling to hisjat proprietor, and he has a ser-
vile eye and a well-drilled smile, and an all-over
look of satisfied abasement. He seems to enjoy
his billet as a male housemaid and a he-girl-of-all-
woi’k, and he touches his hat with his forefinger
and bows and uses the term “ sir ” more than any
other party on the face of the earth. Whether
this alleged animal is born in Australia we cannot
say, but we don’t think he is, and we trust in
Providence that he is not, and if we are wrong on
this point, then we can only wish that we had
been more careful in the selection of a birthplace,
for to be born in the same continent with a flun-
key is too much. Yet if we know the Australian
aright, he is not the kind of individual to white-
wash his head and make an Aunt Sally of himself
for the public amusement, and if he ever learns
this art we devoutly hope that some reliable
foreign tyrant may come along and pump the
paint off his dishonored cranium, and confiscate
the elephant’s-breath coat and the giraffe-sneeze
boots, and then kick him right out into the wil
derness and leave him there to pursue some hones
oalling by means of a bush harrow and an axe.

Deal- Bulletin, —T have read your edify-
ing anecdotes concerning the unoleaniiness of
Chinese cooks, and now tor a word apropos. The
Chinaman is not the only person who chews bread
and spits it out again for the purpose of making
dainty dishes. On board English sliips the sailors
generally have a dish known as “ dog’s-body,”
and this is how it is manufactured ; The pea-soup
comes to the forecastle in a small wooden tub
called a “kid.” Then each sailor takes his share
in a tin-plate, and, getting a biscuit, commences
chewing it and dropping it into the soup, which,
when it becomes thick enough, lie cats. - Kamhlkh,
# # #
Editor Bulletin. —Sir,— l have noticed
several letters in The Bulletin concerning the
culinary methods of Chinese cooks. \Vc hare
one of those individuals here—and for stoning
raisins, I will back him against any living man
not born in China. His plan is to take a great
mouthful of “plums” (just as a man fills his
mouth with tacks when putting up lining on a
wall) and get rid of the stoned fruit as quickly
as his hands can take it out of his teeth. He is a
very clean Chinaman, for he is always careful to
drop the raisins into a dish of fresh water and
blow the stones out into his apron (he invariably
sits down when engaged in this class of work.) I
may add that lie is my boss’s cook and that I don’t
board there. Yours truly, Alphabet.
* #
In Yurrup and America it is the fashion
this season for women to be tall and stately,
whereas last season it was the fashion to be small
and fairy-like, in consequence of which variations
there is much agony in the best society. For the
woman who was short last year can’t be made any
longer this year, unless she is dislocated on a rack
or hung up by the hair with a dining-table or a
brass bedstead suspended to her feet to drag her
out, and nobody knows what to do. The gladsome
angel who brightens man’s pathway and strands
him with her millinery bill is not built like a
telescope to draw m and out with three slides,
neither can she go up and down like a thermo-
meter. The only thing she can do is to wear
springs on her boots and expand her hat, and if
she is so small that even these devices have no
effect, she lies low and waits for better fashions.
* * *
At the recent inauguration of the Lang
Statue it was a matter of general comment that
the men whom the Doctor made wealthy and their
descendants should have failed to subscribe the
£3OOO required for the memorial. It was plain,
from what one heard while walking round the
crowd that many of the old people present were
there because the Doctor had married them;
they thought after all the years that had elapsed
that they owed his memory a debt of gratitude.
Marriage isn’t always a failure.
* * #
There is a certain pleasant little residence
in a lane off Bourke-street, Melbourne, where a
policeman gets killed or else badly damaged now
and then, and consequently the average “cop”
jpves it a wide berth. There are about sixteen
inhabitants as a rule, and about sixteen of them
are drunkards, and the other sixteen are thieves
>,nd assorted malefactors. Now and then some
harmless stranger drifts into this establishment,
and then the painted harridan in charge makes
him drunk on muddy rum at 3s. 6d. a liquor, and
some fusty individual cleans him out at euchre or
else simply shakes out his pockets, and finally
someone else takes his coat and hat and kicks him
on the head and throws him out. In short, it is a
nice place generally, but anyone who passes by
when the door is open can see glaring on the wall
of the front room the legend “ God bless our
home,” and a policeman who went in recklessly to
inquire about something, and came out hurriedly
through the window, taking the sash along with
him, reports that the principal decoration inside
is an illuminated card with the inscription “ Him
that cometh unto me I will in no wise cast out.”
Piety is a great institution.

THE DAY WE USED TO CELEBRATE.
Valentine’s Day ! yet the postmen
Scarce note an increase in their load ;
We always sent one (same as most men)
To some “ pass ng fancy’s ’’ abode.
Ah ! what has become of the Circes
Who simpered, or tossed up the head
At the sight of our tokens and verses ?
They are married, or dead.
Is the world any wiser or better
Because at old customs it mocks—■
At the gloves sent with never a letter,
Or the trinket enclosed in a box?
For Maud’s little “ fives and three-quartbrs ”
We deemed it a pleasure to pay—
But our sons send no gloves to her daughters
On Valentine’s Day.
Such follies have dropped out of fashion
(Tis so much the better or worse),
And prose is the language of passion
Which Strephon once put into verse.
Is the sentiment nowadays deeper ?
Does the tide of true love never ebb ?
Why, the girls, we imagine, get cheaper
Each fourteenth of Feb.
Valentine’s Day ! e’en the trade is
In “ ugly ones ” languid and faint;
Our boys and their knowing young ladies
Care nothing at all for the Saint;
Though he played a great part in the mashes
Of men who are bald on the head.
And those darlings, in muslins and sashes,
Who are married, or dead.
Melbourne.
E.F.
Pie, G.0.M., denial’s vain,
You fickle, faithless fellah,
Have you forgotten “Ragged Jane?”
Has rnem’ry banished “ Stella ? ”
Why thus insult their harassed hearts
With false asseveration?
Or is it that your treasured “ tarts ”
Now’ve lost their fascination ?
Were they all angular and jaggbd
And sour and grim as well as “ragged?”

* * *
Ex-cricketer Lord Harris, the Governor of
Bombay, has ordered that civilians attending his
levees must wear white or coloured trousers with
their dress-coats. This comes of sending an
athletic young man to rule an Indian province.
Harris long ago exhausted all the governing that
had to be done, and now, with nothing in. parti-
cular to put his name and seal to, nor any founda-
tion-stones to lay, he has to bring his massive
brain down to the subject of breeches.
# # *
Melbourne papers report the glad news
that matters are beginning to go better in the
hospital down there. There was trouble for a long
time between the male and female students, and
in the dissecting-room especially the latter used to
burst in and grab things in a way that aggravated
the unconsidered male animal intensely. They
invariably rushed the cancers and the malignant
tumours before any other fellow had a show, and
by shoving violently to the front they used to
secure the inside of the “ subject,” and leave the
lie-student to gaze with a disgusted eye at one leg
and an ear—they cleaned out all the rest. Then
it was objected that the female operators neglected
the small courtesies of the profession—that when
they had finished inspecting a stomach or some
i ither trifle they refused to pass it on to the man
who was waiting for it, and that in such small
matters as an exchange of a plate of brains, or a
fractured skull, or a spine, or a shovelful of
entrails they were lamentably deficient in ordinary
politeness. Very often a pale, spectacled youth
would ask a lady, in the most courteous manner
possible, to kindly pass him two feet and a liver,
and by way of response she would turn her back
on him with derision, but if he had the feet and
she wanted them she would state audibly that be
was no gentleman. However, as already men-
tioned, things are going better now, and though
relations are still more or less strained it is
believed the parties will pull through without
banging each other with spines and umbrellas and
things as they once threatened to do. A sort of
armed neutrality lias taken the place of open
warfare, and the two sexes pass the ribs and the
lungs to each other in freezing silence.
# * #
Miss Lucy Coppin (a daughter of old
George C.) jumped off the pier at Sorrento the
other day into 25ft. of water after a little ghd who
had fallen in. There were several gentlemen pre-
sent who gave her a large assortment of most
excellent advice, and they also looked out to see
that the sharks didn’t bite her. Likewise the
little girl's father sat down on the pier and began
taking off his boots, but Alias Lucy was in the
water and out again in a twinkling.
* * *
In Melbourne District Court on weekly
fraud-summons day the civil servant comes up as
regularly as clockwork. These regular incomes
for a puddle-horse job, round and round, are the
ruin of a man. The system fosters the craven
spirit of always waiting for dead men’s shoes. As
promotion depends almost entirely on merely
keeping alive (for they don’t ap’pint Maloney and
Patsy and Tim so often in Victoria now), the ill-
ness of a superior is watched with glee—all that
delight which Hawthorne’s Chillingworth, in the
“Scarlet Letter,” displays over the slow wearing
away of his enemy. This fiendish spirit pervades
the whole Service, and when you ask a superior
officer about his health he glares as George
Grossmith used to on his understudy, Frank
Thornton.
# # #
Already indignation is being expressed at
the Kintore trans-continental picnic-and-circus-
party’sexploring trip being run on the “never ”
otherwise at State expense. The affair is utterly
senseless at the best, but if Kintore had offered to
pay the expenses connected with his latest fad
nobody could have complained. It is now stated
that there is a difference of opinion amongst the
Playford Ministry. Some of the Ministers wanted
to refuse to pay expenses, but they were over-
ruled by the “loyal” section, who are the
majority.

# # «■
It has just been remarked that the
Ministry, judges, and even the Governor’s women-
kind were sufficiently wily to keep away from the
inauguration of Melbourne Cathedral, and several
of the bishops, in a burst of candour, admitted
that they found the Service “ very beautiful,” but
rather “trying.” The poor children of grace
were worse off than the newspaper reporters, who
“did” the ceremony in sections. As each man
tottered out of the tabernacle exhausted a fresh
victim took his place.
* # #
A well-educated young lady lately answered
an advertisement which appeared in a Brisbane
paper for a “companion to a lady’s daughter,”
resident on a Western station. She was quite
captivated with the kindly reception she received
from the “mother,” and promptly closed with the
offer, subject only to the approval of her parents,
who, being more versed in the snares and pitfalls
of this world, prosecuted private enquiries and
discovered that the advertiser was a brothel-
keeper in a Western town and a well-known fraud
in the matter of decoy advertisements in the
Northern press.
# * *
Mr. Fred. Gibson, barrister-at-law, has
been appointed an acting judge of N.S.W. Dis-
trict Court. It is just aliout time for the District-
Court to be put on a decent footing by the appoint-
ment of young judges, and the institution of a
proper retiring system (based on pension or other-
wise) for judges. Most of the judges who- have
served of late years were appointed when past
middle age, and as they are not allowed to retire
after fifteen years’ service, like the Supreme Court
judges, they slave on for twenty years in order to
be able to claim pension. There can be no valid
reason urged against the appointment to such
posts of young and energetic men. Mr. Back-
house, appointed when he was three-and-thirty,
is perhaps the most popular judge who has yet
sat on the District Court bench as, indeed, he is
one of the most efficient. Judge Murray, too, is
a pronounced success.
* # *
Two Melbourne hawkers have been lined
for offering ice-cream for sale in the yard of St.
Paul’s Cathedral, just previous to morning service.
A constable pointed out to them that people
didn’t usually care to pray on top of ice-cream,
but the hawkers protested that Robert knew no-
thing about the mysteries of Divine worship,
whereupon he impounded them and their cans in
the name of Divine Providence.

The Queensland Cooks’ Union does not
approve of the discharge of Chinamen at 40s. in
favor of white women cooks at 20s. Their secre-
tary cites an instance where the Chinese cook and
butcher, whose joint yearly salary amounted to
£l5O were replaced by a white man and wife at
£OO ! One thing can safely be said in favour of
the Chinaman cook. He is so clean !

Dear Bulletin, — Are pubs, for the public
or the publi-can ? A Newcastle, N.S.W., citizen
recently visited East Maitland. On arrival, he
naturally went for a wash. But the affable bar-
CLERICAL AMENITIES.
WESLEYAN PARSON : “ You see, my friend, they are just beginning to understand
Wesleyanism in Scotland now.”
MAC SCOTT (a Presbyterian) : “On, aye, it’s weel they understand it somewhere: pity they

man told the traveller, who was quite able and
willing to pay for anything he ordered, to “ go
outside and catch a shower if you want a wash.”
Disgusted at this reply to a simple request, the
Newcastle man went to another house where, at
1,15 p.m. he wanted some luncheon, but was
politely informed that “dinner was over —how-
ever, he could have some beer if he liked!”
Now, dear Bulletin, 1 would ask you what are
pubs, licensed for? The sale of grog only, or the
accommodation of the public ? —W.
# # #
The two Perth daily journals are well
represented in the new Parliament of W. A. The.
morning daily (West Austhalian) has its two
proprietors well to the front, Hackett being
appointed to the Council, and Harper securing a
seat in the Assembly. The evening paper (Daily
News) is a limited liability concern, and no less
than three of the partners are in the Lower
House.
♦• # *
“ More especially do we wish to express
onr deep sense of gratitude to thee ” (Oh, Lord !)
“ for coming so soon into the midst of us.” This
is not a prayer to the Almighty, as might Vic
imagined from its style. It is simply a slab of
the address presented to Lord Jersey by the
Goulburn Presbytery. 'The whole congregation
will now join in singing with a loud voice that
beautiful and appropriate hymn. “Nearer, my
Lord, to Thee."
* *
Bishop Barlow, of North Queensland, in
accepting his see—“ For the last month I have
prayed daily this might not be laid upon me, but
feeling that it is a duty I dare not refuse, I will
devote my life to North Queensland and the ser-
vice, of God.” In other words, the good Bishop
is going to keep his attention fixed on Hell and
Heaven.
# * #
A lady who has set up in London as a
fashionable beautifier says that when a new
female client comes along the first thing she does
is to get her face clean, and, as a rule, it takes a
week of persistent labour to do it. There is an
old, forgotten im mstation of paint and powder
and enamel that is as hard and tough as the shiny
exteriorof an ancient rooster’s legs, and for yearsthe
patient has been washing the mere outside of this
alluvial deposit under a mistaken impression that
she was washing her own skin. As to what kind
of a woman will be found underneath that is all a
toss-up ; the outside may be a faded harridan of
50, and the inner surface a person of 30; or, on
the other hand, she may start to wash a com-
paratively young lady and unearth a Methusaleh
who seems to have been leftover from the Deluge.
But, anyhow, she always washes her clients and
keeps them washed, and her whole after-treatment
consists in giving them bottled wiiter in order to
prevent them using other cosmetics. They pay
immense prices for this process, too, and yet if
they only met together and pumped on each other
the result would be just the same.

# * *
Mr. Finney, who disappeared so mys-
teriously and with so loud a splash from a Manly
wharf on a recent dark night, and who has now
so comfortingly turned up in Maoriland, will per-
haps know better next time he disappears. No-
body for an instant believed him to be drowned.
He left a coat behind him, but not a hat. Next
time Mr. Finney disappears he will be so good a 6 *
to take a soft hat in his trousers’ pocket and
leave a hard hat behind on the wharf.
*# • &
The hours imposed on Sydney tramway
officials apparently compel them to gobble up their
meals in the cars. They never get “ smoke-oh ”
at all. Yet, for every vacancy in the department
there are a hundred or more applications. The
attraction must be not the living, but the livery.
Brass buttons always dazzle tin* women. On the
big ocean steamers the officers call this female
weakness “ the yellow fever.”

The best way to make high-class imported
beer is to take the ordinary local article and soak
beef in it. Then when the beef is saturated to
rags you bottle the compound and put a British
label on it, and there you are.

mandments aren’t what they are cracked up to be.

moments, are so hungry for religious consolation
and so afraid of going to hell.

* * #
A bush parson, up Inverell way, having
advised the serving-girls of his flock so to attire
themselves that in future they would not be mis-
taken for their mistresses, one girl yelled, with a
toss of the head and a shrug of the shoulders:
“ Mistaken for the mistresses, indeed ! I’d like to
be mistaken for mo missus. Why, the night of
the bachelors’ ball she was away outside walking
for six hours with three or four swells, and master
locked her up for a week in the bath-room when
•die came home.”
# * #
There is to be seen frequently parading
Hyde Park and the Domain, Sydney, a pic-
turesque old gentleman with white hair and a
pinky cold-weather complexion, who might fit-
tingly sit as a model for the Genius of Benevo-
lence. He is a veritable Casby—unshorn. This
enchanting old fraud is a perpetual snare to the
deadbeats, who, once and once only, plumb the
well of his sympathy. A “bummer” approaches
him with a cunningly-woven yarn of woe, and
ends by .begging to be saved from starvation.
“Do you drink ? ” enquires the bland Casby. If
the mendicant says “ No, sir,” the philanthropist
smilingly replies—“ Then you don’t require me to
give you threepence for a long beer,” and marches
off serenely. If, on the other hand, the beggar
confesses to taking an occasional “snifter,” the
eyes of Casby turn grave and his voice is heard
solemnly declaring—“ My poor fellow, I have
made it a strict rule of my life never to assist
anybody who drinks.” And so he passes on, un-
scathed by the guerilla onslaughts of the cadgers.
# * *
Mr. Horbury Hunt, a Sydney architect,
who prepares the plans and specifications of his
own hats, has just given his brother professionals
a nice “turn.” He told the N.S.W. Public
Works Committee that about 44 architects out of
50 would submit plans which they knew could
not be erected for the amounts specified.

Yankeeland now has over 167,000 miles of
railway.
February 6 was the 40th anniversary of
Black Thursday.
It now appears that the Sioux trouble has
arisen through the whites stealing—“resuming”
II million acres—half of the tribe’s territory.
The present Salvation Army dissensions
may expose General Booth’s method of dealing I
with the funds of the Army. However, no dis-
closure will much surprise people, for in Booth’s
struggle with the brewers over the lease of the
Eagle Tavern, London, the presiding judge
declared his (General Booth’s) sworn affidavit to
be “disingenuous, misleading, and framed to
deceive the Court.” The decision in the case
meant a loss of £20,000 to the Army.
All work has been stopped at Cardiff by a
strike. This is at the Bute docks, “owned” by
Lord Bute, and which cost him two millions of
pounds. Cardiff now does more export trade
than either London or Liverpool, and, in exports,
stands second to New York amid all ttie cities of
the globe, the Cardiff output of coal alone being
over ten millions of tons annually. Yet all this
business revolves around an idle lord, and its sole
aim is to keep him in luxury. A knowledge of
this will feed the flames of the toilers’ enthusiasm
in the coming desperate struggle, when Australia
must again help the dockers.
Miss Susan Minerva Train, only daughter
and eldest child of our old friend George Francis |
Train, was married recently to Philip Dunbar |
Gulager, chief clerk of the gold department of the |
sub-treasury in New York. Susie is no chicken j
now. She was to have been a boy, and then, as a ■
man, President of the United States, for when
the interesting event was expected did not George
pack off Mrs. G.F.T. from Melbourne to Yankee-
land so as to have the expected heir duly qualified
for presidential honours? the “schemes of
men and mice gang aft agley,” and when little j
Susie made her appearance George came in for a
full measure of chaff. However, who knows that
a descendant of G. F. Train may not after all
come to reside at the White House ?
The Czar has a newspaper of his own and
reads it himself—in fact, he is the only individual
who ever does read it. The censor gets all the
Russian newspapers and a good many from other
parts of Europe, and clips out all the articles
which are filled with reverent gush concerning his
huge, ignorant monarch; and then this combined
information is set up as a separate paper and His
Majesty gets his knowledge out of it. Nothing
that can possibly offend the Czar is ever inserted,
and as most things that happen are calculated to
offend the Czar, there are very few facts that he
ever hears about. But, on the other hand, he
hears about a great many facts that never hap-
pened at all, so matters are about even.

The Referee.
“Augur” Chapman, sporting editor of the
Australasian, is, we regret to hear, seriously ill. Mr.
Chapman is one of the few Australian sporting writers
who understands "his subjects, writes good English, has
tho. confidence of all classes, and brings to bear upon
horse-topics the experience of a cultivated man of the
world.
* * *
Handicapper Barnard, Melbourne, retires after
this season, having rendered 21 years honorable service.
This Australian Rous has run the gauntlet of metaphorical
whip-cuts from all the owners, beginning with Tait, De
Mestre, Hurtle Fisher, Winch, Lang, and Waldock, down
to White, Wallace, and our generation, hut comes out
well—w'e only recall one big mistake, which was when ho
let Lee’s Barbelle in so light at Flemington as to make
her a gift of two races in one day. “The General who
never made mistakes never made war,” said Wellington.
* # *
It is proposed to put the succession to Handi-
capner Barnard, Melbourne, in commission, with a com-
mittee of three, but we venture to say the outcries
would be far greater than over the committee of one,
and, besides, there is an immensely greater probability of
improper influence creeping in. Barnard was appointed
after all these things had been tried, and after a great
row about the judge being discovered to have dabbled in
a bet. One would like Augur Chapman to have had a
try at weighting, but this universally honoured turfite is
not so robust as he was.
* * #
By the same mail a Chinese seas-skipper re-
ceived notice that he was winner in the Manila lottery
and of his wife’s mother's death. Great Scot, what luck !
* # *-
The majority of Sydney’s sporting writers and
editors have been insolvent, some of them returning from
King-street without “ whitewash.” This comes of backing
their own tips.
# # #
Jacques Ibbott, the athlete who was ass enough
to ruin his constitution, two or three months ago, by
going without food for forty days, met Pahlo Fanque, the
burly dark-skinned blacksmith, at the Australian Ath-
letic Club, Sydney, the other evening for a small purse in
agoto a finish. Ibbott weighed only lOst. (he used to
weigh twelve), and was giving two stone away. Game as
a pebble, but weak as a cat, the fruit-eater went calmly to
sleep in the second round, and steak was once more
triumphant.
* # *
Wirth’s, Carver’s, and other riding shows are
challenging Australian boundary-riders to sit buck-
jumpersagainsttheircowboys. “Boundary-riders’’arenot
necessarily bold horsemen ; they are, in nearly every case,
fellows who ride about on sleepy old crocks and wouldn’t
get on a “ pig-jumper ” even for a year’s pay.
* * #
The sculling match between Messrs. G. Ruwald
and G. C. Bastes on the Parramatta, from Uhr’s Point
to the Brothers, for a £lO trophy, was won by the former,
somewhat easily, by three lengths. Time for about three
miles, probably somewhat less, 22min. 361 sec.
# # #
The Sydney athletes were fairly successful at
the Maoriland amateur champion sports) In the absence
of Hempton, “Billy” M’Pherson, with the wind astern,
won the 100yds., it is said, in 9 4-sths secs, time, the
correctness whereof it is easy to doubt; the 250yds. in
24 3-10ths secs, (the world’s best record, if correct), on a
slightly-down-hill course, and the 440yds. in 50 2-sths secs.
Bailey, Sydney’s walker, was disqualified twice for
“lifting.” In the absence of Morrison, D. Wood, of
Canterbury, won the half-mile, mile and three-mile races,
the latter in lOmin. 40 2-6ths secs., an Australian record.
Barger won the hurdle races, as usual, in fast time.
#- ‘ *
There were enough stiff ’uns at Rosehill pony
races, the other day, to turn the course into a good-sized
cemetery, had they all been “planted,” but they escaped,
bar two.
# * #
The betting on the Pace Welter at Warwick
Farm was “ very, very funny.” At one time the guileless
public took 2 to 1 about Porridge, and 6 to 1 about Cobar,
but at the finish 100 to f> could have been obtained about
either, and one well known bookmaker offered to lav 100
to 5 the pair coupled.
