THE PLOT OF THE PEDS.
Dear Bulletin, —For ways that are dark and
tricks that are vain, the “ ped.,” professional or amateur,
“ takes the tai l.” In a populous township of the Cabbage
Garden, a ped. by name, say, Muldoon, had hidden his
light under a bushel for some considerable time, and his
sold yearned to “ take down,” as he phrased it, someone,
particularly another “dark horse,” yclept Smith, who
had a local reputation. Smith, however, was by no means
anxious to tackle Muldoon. Hints, challenges, and even
offers of a nominal start had failed to bring him up to the
mark. He wouldn’t bite.
A brilliant idea entered Muldoon’s head, ai d in
company with a mate named M’Pherson, lie daho-
rated it till it became a real work of art. 1 On
the next pay-night (I forgot to mention that all three
worked at the same tannery), when all hands (about 40)
vno 3 1,1 ' , ' le t° wns hip and, of course, near or in the pub.,
M Pherson should take up Muldoon’s challenge, which
would be issued in a drunken mood, and run him for £6,
receiving 6yds. in 100. If possible, Smith was to be
appointed stakeholder. M’Pherson should win, and it
was calculated that this might have the effect of inspiring
•smith to run Muldoon himself. The stakes, though
posted, were only to he nominal, and each party after the
match was to draw his fiver. The challenge was duly
thrown out and accepted, and the stakes posted in Smith’s
nanus, for a match to he run on the following Sunday
morning, thus giving the men a week to get ready.
True to his instincts, Smith entertained grave doubts
as to the genuineness of the affair, for M’Pherson
was cleemed an utter novice on the cinder-path,
ana he bluntly approached Muldoon, expressing his
candiU though unflattering opinion, and proposing
another finale to the sprint to that originally intended.
» was agreed, after some discussion, that Smith and Mul-
midu Sh()ul , ( , l coalesce for the purpose of "putting”
1 herson through. Muldoon could win the match, and
if M’Pherson cared to back his chance (as, of course, Jie
would, believing it a “ oronk go ”) he should be accommo-
dated to the firm’s last shilling. There was a £2O-uote in
it, and they could afford to stand by the original agree-
ment and hand him back his stake.
When approached on the subject, M’Pherson modestly
expressed his doubts as to his ability to win, hut always
wound up by announcing his intention to have a d —d
good try. He’d talk of his schooldays (fifteen years back),
what he could do then, and scouted the idea of Muldoon
“ giving him six yards and losing him,” which opinion
was expressed,to him with great freedom and strong lan-
guage by all and sundry. lie played his part well, never
acting as if he had the matter already squared with Mul-
doon. However) the “other cliajjs” let the match severely
alone. “ Never back anything that speaks,” was chalked
up all over the place, and the warning was taken. Go-
betweens, however, succeeded in bringing the represen-
tative of the hidden firm of Smith & Muldoon on the one
hand, and M’Pherson on the other together, and the
reckless way in which the latter hacked himself gave
intense satisfaction to the two dark peds.
Well, to the race. They toed the mark on the Sunday
morning, bang went the pistol, away they went and as
they started so they finished, M’Pherson winning easily,
despite Muldoon’s best efforts.
Then there was weeping and gnashing Of teeth re-
criminations and abuse, blows and a fight when the clever
partners counted up their losings (£4O) to M’Pherson, the
supposed duffer, followed by an offer from him to run
again level for another £2O aside, which was not accepted.
M’Pherson left the mill the following week, and shortly
after won a big Sheffield handicap off 3 yds. in the
metropolis, under the name of , but you know the
man. —Cosmopolitan.
# # #
Slade, the Maori pugilist, seems to have ended
his professional career in Salt Lake City, where, it is said,
he married three sisters who were very wealthy. After
he had been wedded some time he intimated to his harem
that he wished to take a fourth wahine, but the idea was
distasteful to his three better halves, and, though a
fighting-man, he thought it wise to let the matter drop.
Who would go through life with a tinted
nose when the blood can be cooled by Marshall’s
Tonic N ervine?
R. E. Bush, the Murchison (W.A.)
squatter, who lias just been appointed to the new
Upper House of that colony, is a brother of W.
Bush, the late well-known English wicket-keeper.
Last year’s export of gold from W.A. was
nearly seven times the quantity sent away in 1888.
For the last three years the figures are;—1888,
3,493 ounces; 1889, 15,492 ounces; 1890, 22,810
ounces. Average value, £3 17s fid per ounce.
Lengthened experience in hotel accom-
modation qualifies us to recommend Hurley’s
Royal Hotel, Bathurst, as a hou-e'Sn which one
may enjoy a good time without being squeezed
dry when settling time comes. The house con-
tains 76 rooms, all fitted up and furnished with
the latest conveniences and comforts. Ventila-
tion has been made a study, and the kitchen is
situated in a detached building, thus removing
the unpleasant odours and cockroachy visita-
tions that are apt to emanate from indoor cookery.
The sample rooms are very large and roomy, and
the stables are particularly well-situated and
appointed. Altogether, the hotel will bear
favourable comparison with any house in Aus-
tralia, and that’s a large order.
Jeffrey Rivers is a bewildered negro,
resident in Sydney, and rejoicing in the possession
of a white wife, from whom he has be n living
apart. A babe arriving in port, Mrs. J. R.
sought the offices of the court to compel the
darkey to support it, but, as it was white as
alabaster with ginger hair, Jeffrey Rivers strongly
objected. There was a second male individual
mixed up in this matrimonial embroglio and he
was a Jersey blonde, with hair very much of the
same colour as that thatching the head of Mr. C.
Delohery, D.S.M., the presiding magistrate. The
beak remarked that matters seemed a bit mixed,
but the protesting mother declared that
her propensity for “ eating lemons ” ac-
counted for the darkey’s progeny being
white. The negro declared he was not
fool enough to swallow any such miracle, and
Delohery backed him up by declining to make
an order. This case reminds us of the Rockhamp-
ton white woman who gave birth to a coal-black
piccaninny with a woollen fez. Her hubby, being
a dull-witted white man ready “ to do his duty in
that state of life,” &c., was easily persuaded that
the progenitive phenomenon w; s due to the fact
that h ; s wife had recently been “ drinking too
much coffee.” Which again reminds us of areal
freak of maternity that is on unchallengeable
record. A lady residing in Newington Causeway
(London), some 40 years ago, gave birth to a
female child that was as black as indigo. She
could only explain the matter by saying that, a
few weeks before, she had received a sudden
fright from a negro, who had sprung out of a door-
way and demanded alms of her. This was con-
sidered to Vie a very ingenious lie, of course, and
her husband went frantic with jealousy and
liquor. However, the dark pigment in the skin
gradually paled, and, within six weeks the baby
was as fair as mummery. She grew up into a
beautiful girl (the family came out to Australia),
and is now the handsome wife of a well-known
Melbourne citizen.
Have you heard of Maoe’s Maori.
From the land of fish and kauri,
How in pleasant paths he wanders,
And his tin like water squanders ?
What a splendid hit he’s made?
Tommy Slade.
How he went to Salt Lake City,
Saw three sisters, young and pretty,
Wed them all, and was in clover,
All his days and nights, moreover,
Wears a silk hat on his head,
Tommy Slade.
Once he in a nikau whare,
On a fern couch used to tarry,
Dressed in costume much like Adam
Or Eve, ere they called her madam,
Now in finest tweed arrayed,
Tommy Slade.
Once he by a kopa Maori
Ate his tukaus and porori;
Thought the young kotiros pretty,
Chanted them his wild love-ditty.
Now he has through reinga strayed—
Tommy Slade.
Lately made he fearful blunder ;
Though a “pug.,” he near went under—
Saw a maiden, young and blushing—
Told his love in language gushing,
Told his wives he wished to wed—
Tommy Slade.
Then the storm came down with fury,
He was tried by female jury,
Called a “ savage Maori joker,”
Got much broom-end mixed with poker,
Now he is a husband staid—
Tommy Slade.

Sir Maurice O'Connell married the daughter of

“Charley Flax”: Can sympathise with you; have also

means by characterising the policy of the Canadian
Opposition—full trade reciprocity with the United States
—as “ veiled treason” is this: that whereas freetrade
between Canada and the States would tend to make them
into one people, the retention at the same time of the
Canadian tariff against England would tend to separa-
tion. In this matter Premier Macdonald is simply fighting
against Nature and the inevitable. [Many replies held
over.]
What a row there will be when those
(apart from the promoters &e., who probably
know all about it) interested in certain huge Sydney
land-schemes become aware that Richardson and
Wrench’s valuations of the properties which are
supposed to represent the money invested, show
the said properties to be worth only a third of the
Sums alleged—and when the balance sheets of the
said companies, with countless thousands of
liabilities on the credit side, come under the
microscope!
Last Saturday about thirty gentlemen
drove from the Land Company of Australasia’s
Bridge-street offices, in two four-horse drags to the
Gore Hill Brickworks. The party was accom-
panied thence by Mr. Wm. Harris, managing
director, and Mr. W. C. Costello, assistant
manager, and on arrival at Gore Hill were shown
over the works by Manager A. H. Codings, who
explained the different processes by which the
shale is reduced to the consistency of snuff ere
being damped, and put under ' the terrible
pressure which forms the brick and pre-
cedes its introduction to the kilns. The best
bricks, it seems, are, however, hand-pressed, and
when wanted for facing purposes, burned in the
ordinary furnaces which consume three times the
fuel necessary to bake an equal number of bricks
in the Hoffman kiln. The latter, said to be the
largest kiln in all Australasia, covers an area of
ground 280 ft. long by 80ft. in breadth, containing
10 chambers, and capable of turning out 25,000
bricks per day. Here it may be said that’the
works turn out, as a rule, 300,000 bricks per
week and that they supplied the material
for the Woy Woy tunnel and the Australia Hotel.
The Hoffman kiln was first lighted nearly three
years ago and. kept constantly burning until
ober last, when, the company haying a big
stock of coal on hand, it became more profitable
to sell Ilian to burn it. Now coal is reduced in
price, ana a., die employees—nearly 200 in num-
ber —have returned to work. Inspection over,
the party proceeded to the upper story
of the great kiln, whence a magnificent
panorama was visible outside and a gorgeous
champagne turkey luncheon, within. The various
toasts were drunk with great “enboozeiasm,” and
the individual who, in proposing “the ladies”
started off with “selection before survey,” and
finished up with a eulogium on “ well-pressed
bricks,” was unanimously voted a humourist of
the first water.
The popular Mr. G. E. Labertouche,
officer in charge of Imperial pensions, at Sydney,
for the past 20 years, with a salary of £390 a year,
has been suspended pending enquiry into his
accounts.

lecturers, but the mission was a failure. Dailey
on his return said that he could never get any good
of that man Parkes, as he was always hankering

is not recorded.

YOUNG GUIDE : “Jimmy, I’ve struck the softest Juggins yer ever see. Dis here ole man is dear an

The postage between Australia and Eng-
land is now while between our provinces it
costs us 2d. to send a half-ounce letter. Unless

send for Henniker Heaton.
Henry George has resigned the editorship
of the N.Y. Standard to W. T. Oroasdale, who
has in reality been its mainstay for a considerable
time. Viewed from afar, the Standard seems to
have been waning in interest for some time. A
one-stringed musical instrument can scarcely pro-
duce discord, but its music soon becomes mono-
tonous.
While thousands of unemployed are
clamouring for work throughout the province,
Queensland Government is spending £13,000 in
indenting plantation labour from Italy; and
Lecturer Randall is also mustering immigrant
recruits wholesale at 'Ome.
Remarks the Northern Miner :—“ The
black labour party have not lost all hopes of being
able to bring about the repeal of the statutes
against alien immigration. If this were not so
we should not see the unholy alliance between the
separatist agitators within the colony, and the
absentee representatives in London, who are almost
all coloured-labour advocates, and who lose no
opportunity of declaring that Queensland cannot
be settled by white men. It is abundantly clear
that, separation and black labour have not yet
been completely disassociated in anything but
mere name. ”
1 hackeray’s pon-and-pencil sketches of
Barnes Neweome lecturing on the Affections
might be paralleled by Governor Hopetoun lec-
turing the Australian Natives on Commemoration
Day. He admitted that the United States and
France were “Stable Republics,” but remarked
that if a man hints about an empire or a monarchy
in Paris, he will be run in; wherefrom we must
draw the conclusion that anyone who hints an
Australian Republic ought to be run in. But,
then, how were the U.S. and French Republics
started ? How did they reach their present noble
stable state? Did they peacefully grow out of
the Victoria Regina flower of a crown to the
white lilies of the Republic ?
A New York constable, whose business it
is to see ladies over a crossing in Broadway,
says;—
“ I have been here for many years on duty now, and
between you and me the women are getting liner every
day. It’s a fact that they are putting up big muscle
lately. I don’t know whether it’s the gymnasium or beef-
steaks, but where a big and hard arm on a girl was a
rarity ten years ago, it is not a scarce thing now. Did
you ever know that an arm can say a good deal? I
believe there is as much eloquence in a girl’s arm as in
her eyes. Some of them also flirt with their arms. Kow,
I’m a pretty well-battered old chap, but darn me if there
ain’t a lot of giddy things flutter across here every day
that send all sorts of telegraphic signs through their
arms at me whenever I help them over the street.”

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wisli to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to Tub Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contribut ions as under :—
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
/ ( . Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration,
o. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. 'I he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not mereh on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

(FOR THE BULLETIN)
Bold self-seeker, ever pressing
To the land of wealth and fame,
With the pious patter-word upon your lip,
Still the traitor thought caressing—
Masquer in a borrowed name,
Despair, for Xruth. has let her sleuth-hounds slip*
With a Bible in your pocket
You’ve advanced by fraud and trick
To the realm of notoriety and pelf:
He that riseth like a rocket
May come downward like a stick,
For hypocrisy at last defeats itself.
Brute-like courage and endurance
Mix with cunning in your mould,
You have fought ’against fearful odds with might
and main ;
You’ll not fail for self-assurance
But there’s blood upon your gold
And on your brow the awful brand of Cain.
J. LE GAY BRENTON
In consequence of a leak in a cylinder-
cover, the water-supply of Bathurst, N.S.W., was
suspended for three days. But the residents
never knew it, bless yer !
The experience of Police-Inspector Mor-
row, of Walgett, N.S.W., is that “the rising
generation is not so fond of drink as their fathers
were.” Good experience in bad grammar.
Jerome K. Jerome, Louis Stevenson, and
other writers of fiction have just been called upon
to pay the last dread penalty of success. Their
alleged portraits, under the description of “ Popu
lar Authors,” are now to be seen scowling and
squinting at mankind from the blotchy columns
of Melbourne ’Erald, and a vague hope that
literary men were not quite as bad as one might
think has given place, day by day, to the horrible
conviction that such people are a dashed sight
worse than anybody ever imagined. The murderers
and perpetrators of criminal assaults, who in days
of yore almost monopolised the ’Erald’s Chamber
of Horrors, were things of sweetness and light as
compared with these popular authors. Their
faces are balls of evil passion, .and their noses are
mostly indented by the brand of Cain. Narrow
slanting eyes betray their Mongolian origin, and
the way they plaster their front hair upon criminal
heads stamps them as football-barraokers of the
most depraved kind. It were hard to say for
certain which of them is Jack the Ripper, because
there may be more “cuts” to follow. In the
meantime we suggest that every available Popular
Author ought to be arrested on suspicion and
hanged without the option of a fine, because the
world won’t hold enough sin and wickedness to
keep literary genius up to the frightful pitch of
its portraits in the ’Erald.
As a hair dressing and renovator, Ayer’s Hair
Vigor is universally commended. It eradicates
dandruff, cures eruptions of the scalp, invigorates
and beautifies the hair, and prevents its fading or
turning gray.
In some verses headed “ The Native
‘ National Ode— 1891! ’ ” Mr. Unknown-Christian-
Name Freeman has had a fine joke with the Aus-
tralian Natives Ass’n. Ho sent them a rhymed
joke—they missed the fun and awarded the rhyme
a ten pound note. We translate the joke so that
even a native companion may catch on :
Fair Land of Morning, evergreen
(where long-drawn-out droughts ne’er trouble the strug-
gling farmer.)
Dear favoured home of Peace serene
(land of the fiercest industrial conflict the world has
known—land of blackfellovv-battues and the Eureka
Stockade)
Where plenty pours her brimming horn
(on benevolent asylums, refuges, and the Vagrancy Act),
Where flies a flag unstained, untoru
(proudly waving on the anniversary of Convicts’ Day.
Motley is the only wear!)
Hail, brothers, hail !
Where free beneath thy sunny skies
Man may to fullest manhood rise !
Hail, hail, Australia !
The large supply of hail is a joke on the “sunny
skies ” business which also gracefully implies
reference to the people sweetly sleeping nightly in
Sydney Domain and Eitzroy Gardens ; while the
idea of a man rising to fullest manhood in a de-
pendency which cannot keep out Chinese scum
without appeals to a foreign “Privy Council,” is
the unkindest cut of all. Mr. Freeman’s fund of
humour is most graphically illustrated in a line of
splendid alliterative bombast where he invokes
and re-sexes Australia as follows :
“ August old Albion’s gallant son / ”

Parramatta is arching its back and spitting
out protests against being made a central depot
for the reception and maintenance of prisoners,
madmen and mendicants. Parramatta is not yet
fully seized of its natural vocation.
Several times last year the N.S.W*
Supreme Court dealt with attorneys who had re'
tained their clients’ money. Early this year the
Court will be asked to say whether those attorneys
who retain junior barristers’ fees —they can’t get
tick from the senior bar—should not also be dealt
with. In England a solicitor was recently
suspended for 12 months for non-payment of fees
to Counsel. As the attorneys get the money from
their pigeons expressly to pay barristerial fees,
the retention of the fees clearly amounts to a mis-
appropriation of trust-funds.
At a meeting held recently in the East
end of London one frowsy individual, who smelt
like a billiard-room full of stale gin, alluded to
this country six times as “ qur colonial possess-
ions.” Every miserable Britisher who whoops at
a dog-fight in a Whitechapel pub. still fancies that
he owns Australia, and thinks that Parkes and
Higinbotham and Mcllwraith and Atkinson and
everybody else out here are somehow his private
property. And when he hears that N.S.W. or
Queensland is manifesting disloyal sentiments he
invariably states that “ we —which is himself and
the bald-headed man who is drunk under the
table—will have to put this sort of thing down
with a strong ’and.”
Let no one deceive himself. Sydney,
with a sewerage system, has a larger death-rate
from filth-diseases—typhoid, diphtheria, &c. —
than Melbourne without a sewerage system.
Why? Because of the • penurious landlordism
and jerry-plumbing—not Jersey-plumbing, dear
printer!— of the City of the Beautiful Har-
bour. Up to now every landlord has
been able to do in Sydney just as he liked.
Until Sydney has a proper Building Act pro-
viding for rigid inspection and with penal clauses
tacked to it, the Cornstalks will go on Journeying
midden-wafted to Abraham’s bosom in the same
old way. The Australasian Builders and Con-
tractors’ News, which commenced the crusade
against the jerry-plumbers and heartless rent-
gobblers of Sydney, has rendered a service to the
State.

Captain Rossi, of Goulburn Cathedral
notoriety, must at length have reached the height
of his ambition : he has put in the foundations of
about three healthy lawsuits. So careful is the
Captain with his proofs that he got himself
photographed in the reclining position in the
gutter in which he alleges his enemies placed him.
A Parramatta man has been fined 40s. and
costs for attempting to snatch a kiss from a mar-
ried woman —who threw a bucket of water over
him prior to issuing a summons.
Contrary to all railway precedents and
usage, the trains in Queensland are said to run
“up into the country” and “ down to town,
whereas in every other railway system the terms
are ‘‘ up to town ” and “ down into the country.”
The Queensland Railway Commissioners are said
to have been insensibly influenced into this
transposition of adverbs by the familiar habit of
old colonists in alluding to their inland travels as
being “ up the bush ” or “ up country.”
AMERICA’S GREATEST STATESMAN
At one of the large dinners given at the seat of
government lately, the great orator said : “Permit
me, ladies and gentlemen, to say that there is no
drink in the world so conducive to social inter-
course as the famous St. Louis Lager Beer. As
a mental and physical relaxative it has no equal.
It refreshes the body and exhilarates the mind.
It gently moves the blood to accelerated action,
and arouses the more generous sentiments that
cluster around the human heart. Its action in-
spires noble thoughts, and at the banquet table,
with intellect and genius, it touches chords of
inspiration that prompt to lofty efforts.”

Ayer’s Sarsaparilla is the best remedy. It vitalizes
the blood and expels all humors.
UUR OWN INVESTIGATIONS.
FRON the DAILY NORTHERN TELEGRAPH.
Recent editorial comment by some of the most
influential papers in Great Britain, respecting the
value of an article which is extensively advertised,
has led us to make certain investigations on our
own behalf, in order that we might lay before our
readers facts which we had personally verified-
Advertisements are one thing, voluntary
editorial endorsement of the value of an article is
quite another thing. This latter occurs so very
rarely that it becomes all the more important.
From the report below it will be observed that
we have confined our investigations wholly to
local people. The reports are from well-known
citizens, which must carry conviction to the minds
of every thinking man and woman.
Messrs. Jackson and Co., of 9, Regent’s build-
ings, top of Penny-street, being among our leading
merchants, their opinion was first sought by us.
In answer to our inquiry Mr. Jackson states
that the article in question was one of the most
staple. Immense quantities were sold by him
every month, and the actual merit which it pos-
sessed was undoubtedly the cause of its being one of
the leading articles of trade in Blackburn. * * *
Among these cases which Mr. Jackson related
was that of a well-known society lady (whose
name we are not at liberty to publish), who had
been confined to her room for more than three
weeks by muscular rheumatism in the back,
during which time she was under the treatment
of one of our leading medical men ; but continued
from the first to grow worse. She was very much
prejudiced against advertised remedies, and al-
though repeatedly urged to try St. Jacobs oil she
persistently refused. Her sister, unbeknown to
her, procured a bottle of the oil from Messrs.
Jackson. The first application gave the lady
relief, and before the contents of one bottle was
used she was entirely free from pain. She felt so
grateful that she personally visited the store of
Messrs. Jackson, and told them the facts as above
narrated, and says that hereafter she shall advise
all her friends who have rheumatism to dismiss
their doctors and use St. Jacobs oil.
ATEXAS SIFTING.
F. G. Presby, Marshall, Texas, April 14, ’9O,
writes the Waterbury Watch Co. as follows :
“ Though a stranger to you I wish to let you know
something that happened to me in the spring of
1889 on account of one of your Waterbury
Watches. I left my home in Pensacola City, Fla.,
to find a new one farther west. Coming to Den-
ver, Colo., and having travelled all over the
Rocky Mountains without any watch in my
pocket, as I was coming one day from my place to
Denver, Colo., after I had got my place in order,
I came to look over the city. My intention was
to go to Kansas City, Mo. I passed one watch-
maker’s store on Larimer-street, in Denver, and
saw there a watch in the window for only 2dol.
50o.; I went and I bought one, paid the money for
it, and left the city next day for Kansas City,
Mo., in the 1 p.m. train. After I was about an
hour’s ride from the city the conductor of the
train came and took a seat by my side I asked
the conductor the time, and as a gentleman he
told me the time, and I said I also have my watch
to tell me the time. As soon as the gentleman
(conductor) of the train espied my watch he com-
menced to laugh, and told me at the same time he
could get a bushel of such trash as Waterbury
Watches, all he wanted, for one dollar, while
I had paid 2dol. 50c. But I did not lose
my courage, and told the conductor that I
would bet a cigar, only first that I was not a
betting man, and secondly that my Waterbury
Watch for 2.50d0l was just as good as an Elgin
or any other 250.00d0l watch, in regard to keep-
ing good time. The conductor accepted the bet.
Nearing Kansas City next day I notified the con-
ductor about our bet. When we reached Kansas
City my 2.50d0l Waterbury Watch was correct
and the conductor’s watch from Elgin, 111., was
1| minute behind time, so the Waterbury Watch
won the bet. I have received the cigar, have set
fire to it, and am enjoying the smoke.”

The Woman-Preacher's Baby.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Yes, mate, I was here during the great
gold rush and saw the seamy side of diggings’ life
from the start till the excitement died out. We
were a rough lot at Carey’s Flat, a mixture of
all sorts—neither birth nor colour nor creed made
any difference, for the object of pursuit was the
same. We all wanted gold, and knew little and
cared less about anything else.
“Carey s Flat wasnoplacefor a woman, you may
imagine, and yet a woman, a young thing she
was, too, came in amongst that rough lot and
settled down. It was a strange tiling for her to
do, but, then, Janet Ray was a strange woman,
not like anyone I ever saw. She was a preaching-
woman, a regular Dinah Morris, who fancied she
had 1 a call ’ and could do these miners some
good. It was curious to see the effect her preach,
ing had on the men, and the hold she gradually
got of them. She was a working woman, nothing
at all of the fine lady about her, and so timid, you
could scarcely hear her voice when she spoke, if
she wasn’t preaching. But when she stood up in
the long summer evenings, and the men gathered
round her to hear what she had to say, then there
came a change over her. Her voice rang out clear
as a bell, there was a light in her face like that
you might fancy an angel would own, and you
wouldn’t think she was the same creature. I
could never rightly remember what she had been
saying after it was all over, any more than I could
tell you the notes of a bit of soft-played music that
had satisfied my nature, without my understand-
ing it, but I know I used to feel lifted off my feet
as I listened to her, and carried up to some lovely
sphere where I could see green fields and flowers,
and hear birds singing and water sparkling.
That’s how the preaching affected me, and I’d like
to see the one who wouldn’t be made a better man
by having his feelings stirred that way. Generally
the men were very quiet while she was preaching,
as quiet as if they were sitting in a swell church,
with cushions on the seats, and a dim light coming
through the coloured windows. Sometimes there
would be a bit of a row at the edge of the crowd
when the fellows had been drinking, and then
you’d see two or three of them who were listening
to her words jump up and clear off the rowdies in
a twinkling.
* * *
“ There was one big fellow whom we noticed
before long was always first in defending Janet
from any little annoyance—Charlie Strauss, a
good-natured, quiet German, and he was always
nearest to her when she was preaching. He
seemed to be counting every word that fell from
her lips, but was so seemingly content with watch-
ing her that he didn’t much heed their meaning.
We soon saw that Charlie meant to marry the
little preacher, and I don’t think he had much
trouble in persuading Janet, for she had a sort of
fancy for him from the first. We saw them go off
together early one morning, and when they came
back Charlie told me they were man and wife,
having been tied up properly by a bush missionary
who was visiting a town some miles off.
“They settled down comfortably, and seemed to
rub along happily enough until their youngster
was born, and then things changed a bit. From
that time Janet seemed to be off her head. She
took a dislike to the place, and to every soul in it,
even to poor Charlie. She said we were a godless
lot, and declared we were all going stiaight to
perdition. It appeared that she had had a vision,
in which the Lord spoke to her, telling her that He
had sent her this child, who was to be dedicated
to His service. She hugged the boy in her arms
and would not let any of us touch him lest we
should pollute him, and declared that she must go
away with him to some better place where she
could bring him up aright. She said he was des-
tined to be a great preacher, and she was anxious
to lose no time in having him baptised. She was
always trying to get away with the baby, and
Charlie had a time of it looking after her. He
would have gone off with her when he saw how
her mind was set on it, only there was a good
prospect before him of making something out of
his claim after all his months of hard work, if he
only held on a little, and he tried in vain to per-
suade her to wait for a while. He might as well
have talked to his pick. At last he thought if he
could get the child away from her and into safe
keeping somewhere she would, perhaps, fret a
while after it, but would be more content to stay
where she was. Charlie was . as good as half-a-
dozen when it came to a row, but he wasn’t much
of a hand at solving a problem so he made arrange-
ments with the family of a settler living away
back in the bush to take the child, and said he
would send it to them by Fred, a teamster, who
would be travelling in their direction. He got
the baby away from its mother and handed it
over to Fred, who was a careless, drunken savage
and misunderstood things, and thinking Charlie
wanted to be rid of the boy altogether, he pitched
the poor little chap down an old claim on his way
from the diggings, and thought he had done a
good turn. Aye, those were rough days ; human
life wasn’t thought of much account in those
times.
“When Janet found out her loss she rushed about
like as though she were stark, staring mad. It
touched the hardest heart to see the agony in that
poor thing’s face. She didn’t suspect her husband
of having had a hand in the child’s disappearance;
she thought some of the men had taken it to
punish her for the way she had been speaking of
them.
‘ This lasted for some days and then some of the
fellows couldn’t stand it any longer—they told
Charlie they would tell her where the youngster
was, if he didn’t tell her himself. Charlie, poor
fellow, did as he was bid, and the next thing we
saw was him and his wife setting off for the
hut in the bush to bring the little chap back.
“ We never knew what happened when they
reached the hut and found the child wasn’t there;
we only knew that they came back and lived for a
little while in the old place, and that Janet
accused her husband of having destroyed the
child. Nothing could shake her in that belief
and she neither looked at him nor spoke to him
from morning to night.
“ Some men would have killed her, but Charlie
was very patient with her for all his misery, and
bore with her whatever she did.
“At length she disappeared one night. We
searched for her, but couldn’t find a trace of the
poor creature. We thought she had likely fallen
down a claim in her wanderings, and searching
there for her we found the body of her little lost
boy. _ ■
“Nothing was heard of her fate till some months
afterwards, and then her skeleton was found in
the bush where she had wandered off the track
and perished from hunger.
“ Charlie left the diggings. He never could bear
the place after his trouble. I daresay he drank
himself to death long before that poor skeleton in
the bush was brought to light,”
G. B. R.

A Frenchman has hanged himself ufc
Broken Hill, leaving behind a paper bearing the
words :—“ No work, no money, no board, Uo
lodging ; an end with horrors is better than
horrors without end.” Why didn’t he wait fot
that letter on “ The False Fight of Socialism,” in
which the O, Silly-Vane states that the,desire to
live is the main incentive to work. The suicide’s
desire to obtain work should have been strong
enough to sustain his life whether the opportunity
to work was afforded or not. At least that’s the
gospel according to O’Sullivan.
The sentence of death passed on Rodney
Claud Spencer at Port Darwin has been commuted
to imprisonment for life. Spencer is an aristo-
cratically-connected vagabond, who, when he
committed the crime, was a buffalo-hunter in the
Northern Territory. There can be no doubt what-
ever that if the victim had been a white man
instead of a blackfellow, Spencer would have been
hanged, for, according to the evidence, a more
deliberate and cowardly murder was never com-
mitted in this world. Spencer simply shot the
defenceless black in cold blood in his own camp.
Melbourne Land Mortgage Bank, which
borrows London cash at 4 per cent., and ladles it
out locally in sky palaces, etc., declared 12| per
cent, dividend last week, but there is always
some nasty shareholder, with his douche of cold
water, just when the whole meeting is one broad
grin of delight. Thus did one Walker get up,
and suggest that only 10 per cent, should be paid,
because look at the Premier Evanescent, and how
wise it was to have a big reserve fund. But the
Reverend Mr. Fulford was on hand, and oh ! he
did speak ! “Mr. Chairman,” said he, “are we
to turn a banquet into a mere feast ?” Trust your
Fulford.
If Lady Burton, widow of the great ex-
plorer and Oriental scholar, can raise the money,
the last resting-place of her illustrious husband
and herself will be novel and appropriate. Mort-
lake is the spot chosen, and Lady Burton says ;
“ The ground is lift, by Oft. This is to be covered
by a dark stone Arab tent. In the tent, above
ground, are to be two slabs to hold two coffins
(let me have a little place by his side) and room
for a small altar at their head. He is embalmed,
and I shall be, so there is no danger to health.
Outside the tent, above the flap curtain which
serves as door, will be a marble crucifix, under it
a ‘ Book of Life,’ on one side his name and death
recorded, the other page blank for me. tinder
the book will be inscribed Justin McCarthy’s
beautiful sonnet of fourteen lines, which embraces
all his life. The expenses of the interment and
monument will be £390, a very heavy expense for
me in my altered circumstances. Should anything
be done to help I shall be very grateful, but in no
way hurt should nothing come of it.”
Longevity.
It is likely that a tendency to longevity is fre-
quently inherited—that it runs in families. We
know that a lack of vitality, weakness of constitu-
tion and a tendency to certain diseases are trans-
missible. Presuming that nature never indicates
any physiological preference for individuals, We
can account for this principle of preservation by
the fact that in all large families the wbakest and
diseased die out, leaving only the healthy ahd
most hearty to propagate. With such sOuhd
stock, long life through successive generations is
the result; If the truth must be told plainly
death is usually a species of Suicide. People Seeih
to set upon the mode of life which will kill theitl
quickest. Too much eating and tob little slebp-
ing, stimulants, reckless dissipation', brains over-
burdened with business, hearts harrowed With
Oates and responsibilities of life —such are the
things that hurty folks into early graves. Worry
and nervous excitement kill people quicker than
wars and famines; The great fault with man-
kind is that it fails to take notice of the first warn-
ings which nature sends. Let a person suffer a
simple (as she or he thinks) symptom, let it bfe
headache, sleeplessness, indigestion or neuralgia,
it is a common retnark for them to say, “ Oh ! it’s
nothing, let it go as it came;” Such symptoms
always precede serious attacks Of disease* and if
such warnings were appreciated, how much misery,
suffering and even life itself might be saved. A
few shillings invested in two or three bottles of
Clements Tonic will ward off disease, cteate a
healthy apipetite, stimulate the liver and kidneys,
and compel all the organs to properly perform
their functions. Mr. James O’Leary, Goulburn,
(N.S.W.) writes : —“ In 18(35 I was gold-mining in
Victoria and was attacked by typhoid fever which
laid me on a sick bed for seven months; I was
delirious most of the time, so cannot say exactly
what happened to me during that period ; all that
I know is that for seven or eight years afterwards
I was continually in the hands of the doctors, who
somehow did not seem able to grapple with the
peculiarities of my case; they finally advised a
sea voyage. I went to Europe, stayed there some
months, caught a severe cold in the trying English
winter, which turned to pleurisy and laid me at
death’s door for the second time. I was carried
on to the steamer to come to Australia, as I
thought, to die ; however, the voyage revived me
a little and I could walk with the help of a stick.
On arrival at Sydney, I read of the wonderful
effects of Clements Tonic in restoring enfeebled
strength. I got a bottle, felt better, got another,
felt oetter still, then another and another and
kept at it till I had taken ten bottles, and I felt
as if 50 years had been taken off my life ; more
like a man of 30 than ohe of 83. It completely
restored my strength and health and 1 believe it
is the most wost wonderful medicine the world
has seen, for Clements Tonic made a young man
of me, when doctors, their physic and their sea
voyages failed. This is a fact and there is no
denying such facts. I am now 85 years of age,
have married my fifth wife within the last two
years and have a son under six months old, and
hope to live another 20 years. I think that says
something in favour of Clements Tonic.”

ceston, J. ID. Johnstone; and all dealers every-

The Cook’s Horse.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Hr wasn’t our cook; that is, he was not in the
hut, and he wasn’t the shearers’ cook, nor even the
rOuseabouts’ cook. But he was in charge of the
barracks kitchen. The barracks are where the
tangies live, and the rangies are the jackeroos,
which (Bible for this grammar) are colonial ex-
periences, which are pannikin-overseers, which
are relations of the owners, which are which—for
I could replicate ad nauseam. But to our cook.
He came out in a white cap and apron, and
“headed ’em”; he headed’em well, and won a
little. Then he ipade a match at draughts, and
lost five shillings, and the station champion played
him for a fiver with eleven men, and lost, so the
chef was £4 15s to the good in the chequers. He
would not play euchre because the blacks play it,
and he would not play forty-fives because he was
not Irish, and his knuckles couldn’t stand the
bang on the table, but he’d play crib, and he some-
times won, sometimes lost —generally the former :
a quiet, unassuming old sport, who was always
being had by the boys for sixpence or a bob ; but
it takes a lot of sixpences to make a sovereign.
Then a teamster came along just before the
races —the station races, you know—and he had a
lame horse. The teamster called him “Bally,”
because he had a white face. He did not know
the cook, but offered to sell “Bally’’for a fiver,
and let in the poor old cook, who was rather proud
of having a horse.
Now some devil incarnate (taken from the
Snivelling Snooze) filled the old man up with
whisky, and persuaded him to subscribe a pound
and enter “Bally” for divets races at the annual
shearers’ meeting. 1 fancied he entered for every
race; but he insisted oil changing the horse’s
name to “ Ruin,” because he said the races would
ruin him. Gripes! (colonial for Oarambo) it did
amuse us to see the slushy careering about on his
ruin. We did not treat him fairly : we knew his
weakness and got at him with a nobbier or two,
until he had backed “Ruin” to win the Hack
Race, the Handicap, the Blue Gum Stakes and
the Hurdle Race. Then w’e began to think the
toan was mad, that his madness had been caused
by some swindle, and that it was for this reason
he always stipulated “first past the post, play or
pay.”
Then the races came on. I had almost forgotten
to mention that cash was the cook’s motto. He
posted his coin and we had to do the same.
The Bally horse was turned out on Cunning-
ham’s Flat, and was never seen until the night
before the races, when the teamster (who had sold
him to the cook) brought him in, looking well for
a grass-fed horse, but we began to ponder when
the cook removed his shoes. They came off so
nicely that he must have taken off such shoes
before, or else they had been badly put on. At
any rate, “ Ruin ” was in the stall in the morning,
and though plastered with mud he looked well
enough.
Oft the course the cOok was drunk and the
teamster was nearly so, but the latter had appar-
ently become thinner, perhaps through sickness.
They were both taking bets in a reckless manner,
posting the cash. Here the agreeable part of rny
tale ends, for “Ruin” won all his races. Mind
you, he was lame and muddy, but he won, and
the teamster rode him. I fancy the cook and the
teamster won a couple of hundred. They left
after shearing. Perhaps the most extraordinary
part of this yarn is that the “gun-room” people
say that the owner of “ Ruin ” was a d d good
cook and was always up in time. After the man-
ner of ACsop (but he was so long that it puts me in
mind of beer) I give a moral—and here, in paren-
thesis, I may remark that the cook had a moral
certainty. His horse was old “ Hector,” which
(for the sex again, vide Holy Writ) had won—
never mind what he won.
Moral: Don’t trust book or booky by the bind-
ing. This opens a mine of discussion. I have no
time to analyse trust and book and binding.

One Croydon (N.Q.) paper describes its
hated contemporary as: “Our poor, deah, old,
flat-chested, dribble-nosed, gummy-eyed contem-
porary, the Croydon green-gage.” Jack Hoolan,
M. takes credit for the adjectives.
Recently, a labour-agent in Dunedin,
N. advertised for 200 pick-and-shovel men to
proceed to Tasmania. Within two hours the
agent’s office was besieged by 600 navvies jumping
on each other for the chance of getting Is. an
hour.

£j[The Strike Committee of the Central
District Council of Queensland has issued, to

cluding sentence of which reads thus: —“If the
worst comes to the worst, we can always ride

Bill Beacom, known all over the Far North
as “ Greasy Bill,” (he used to boast that he “could
smell gold,”) and who was murdered by Papuan
cannibals on Sud Est Island, New Guinea, some four
months ago, was once an indefatigable prospector
at Charters Towers. Bill (whose nickname was
derived from the fact that when once he put on a
suit of clothes he never doffed them until they
showed symptoms of dropping off him) was work-
ing alone and was murdered principally for his
swag of “ tombaggo.” He was pinned to the
earth with spears, and, while writhing in torture,
his head was cut off. Then his liver and heart
were cooked and eaten by “the Hill” men at a
great roast feast. The news of his murder didn’t
leak out for three months. Then the incensed
miners on Sud Est burned some villages, and, by
threats, obtained from the chiefs the surrender of
the chief murderers, who Were promptly chained
up, and, later on, handed over to Sir William
Macgregor, Administrator of New Guinea. The
miners complain that Macgregor “ sympathises
with the natives, and is treating the miners as
lawless adventurers,” he having refused even to
leave a boat as a means towards their escape in
the event of a native rising. Yet the Adminis-
trator has embalmed his own questionable rush
through the Louisiades group in an official report
to the Queensland Government, as a feat of which
he is extremely proud. Poor old “ Greasy ” had
the reputation of being “ generally the first at a
rush and the longest to stay.”
From Albert Edward’s public reference to
out late lamented Oaringtoti as “my friend of
40 years’ standing,” we gather that the Baron
(who has in all 2400 tenants working for him and
providing him with money) began to shout drinks
for his Royal Nibs at a very early age.

John Vicars and Co., of Sussex-street,
Sydney, dismissed the other day from their wool-
factory three old and capable employes, appar-
ently for tlo Other reason than that they were
prominent trades-unionists and had besought the
firm to ameliorate the Condition Of their work-
people, more especially their child-labourers. The
bull’s-eye bf enquiry has since revealed some ugly
facts. The children in Vicars’ wool-factory work
from 6 in the morning till 6 at night, with a break
of half-an-hour for breakfast and three-quarters-
of-an-hour for dinner, except on Saturdays, which
are half-holidays. All hands in the factory work
60 hours per week, and the children often work
overtime till 9 o’clock at night, some of the
overtime being paid at the rate of one
penny per hour! This tariff is impugned
by John Vicars, but the children them-
selves affirm it. A 12-year-old boy declared
that he worked 15 hours a day—till 9 o’clock p.m.
—when he was on nightwork ; wages, 6s. 6d. per
week ; overtime, Id. per hour. His brother, aged
14 years, received 9s. 6d. per week, and Id. per
hour for overtime, and, during the previous fort-
night had gained 38 pence by such over-perspira-
tion. A 13-year-old lad had achieved sore eyes
over his work, for which he was paid 7s. per week,
and Id. per hour overtime. Another of the same
age “ worked 60 hours a week for 6s. He was in
the drying-loft where the heat was intense, and if
he lifted his head he would bump it against the
corrugated-iron roof. He could sometimes wash
himself in perspiration, and wring it out of his
clothes.” Girls received the same overtime in-
ducements and compulsions. Many of the
children live in distant suburbs and have to rise
before daylight in order to reach their work in
time, as late attendances are punished by fines.
A deputation from the men in the factory was the
prelude to the dismissal of three of their number
—William Welch (President of the Textile
Workers’ Association), 151 years with the firm;
James Beattie (Vice-President of the T.W.A.),
and William Hunter (secretary of do.) The firm
deny that prominent unionism has anything to do
with their dismissals, but it is a curious coincidence
that the three principal officers of the trade-unioni
should be selected as scapegoats to the exigency of
factory interests. All the workers at Vicars and
Co, are under strenuous conditions. They get no
pay for any holiday, nor for any absence in con-
sequence of sickness. The Trades and Labour
Council has promised to assist the Textile
Workers, who are now thoroughly angry. What
is better, the female employes have promised to
statid by the ineti.
Charles Bradlaugh, Atheist, Malthusian,
Republican and Individualist; son of a solicitor’s
clerk, was born in London, September; 1838, and
educated chiefly at the National sOhOol, Bethhal
Green. Errand boy in the office where his father
spent his life, he then became clerk to a cOal-
merchant. At this time, preparing for confirma-
tion, he discovered that the thirty-nine articles
were contradictory. Parsonical suspension conse-
quent thereon from Sunday-school duties and his
defeat in a debate on Biblical inspiration, shook
his faith and he finally became a declared Free-
thinker when he was further confirmed in his
antagonism to religion by the action of the cleric
who got him dismissed from his situation on his
refusal to recant and influenced his parents against
him. At seventeen years of age he left home and
started without capital as a coal dealer, failed,
went hawking braces, and found a home with the
widow of Richard Garble, in whose house he
studied Latin, Greek, Arabic and other tongues.
In debt and hopelessly in love with Hypatia
Garble, he enlisted in the 7th Dragoon Guards,
where his awkwardness made him the butt of the
regiment till he thrashed its bully. A bad horse •
mail, he was sodii appointed orderly-room clerk,
but, inheriting it small sum by the death of ail
aUiit, bought his discharge and went to the aid
of his widowed mother. Again he began at the
bottom Of the laddeir as factotum to a solicitor at
10s. weekly, but was soon promoted to a position of
trust. He then married a Mist Hooper, and, the
tolerance of his employer allowing him to lecture;
quickly made a reputation and was engaged to
deliver lectures whenever legal duties permitted;
His defeat of numerous representatives pf ortho-
doxy, lay and clerical, caused much ill-feeling;
and his life was endangered on various occasions.
He was then articled to a solicitor, with whom he
entered into partnership, but the connectioh only
plunged him into debt. Some attempts were
made from time to time to prevent his
lecturing, but Bradlaugh successfully fought
out the issue in the higher law-courts, and
at the death of John Watts assumed the
secular leadership. Shortly after, his talented
defence of the National Reformer secured a
repeal of certain obnoxious obstacles to publica-
tion. At this period Bradlaugh, though earning
about a thousand pounds yearly, was content to
live in a three-and-sixpenny East End lodging,
devoting his earnings to paying off the liabilities
incurred in the law business and to fighting the
foes of free speech and a free press. Amongst
other things Bradlaugh has been charged with
drawing up the 1867 proclamation of the Irish
Republic. Travelling in defence of liberty
he had many adventures in Italy, France.
Spain, and America, and was once arrested
by the agents of Bomba as a Garibal-
dian, but escaped through his personal daring.
After many vicissitudes and four attempts he was
elected for Northampton, in April, ’BO, and took
his seat on July 2nd of the same year. The
attempts made to exclude him from the House
over the affirmation question and his parliamen-
tary conduct are too well-known to require revival
in these columns, suffice it to say that in Brad-
laugh the world has lost a man who for earnestness,
courage, purity and clear headedness was not
excelled by any of his contemporaries.

The main objects of the Company, briefly stated, are 1. To establish an Irrigation Colony at
Mulgoa, near Sydney, in the colony of New South Wales. 2. To purchase and acquire the rights and
privileges of the promoters, Messrs. George Chaffey and Henry Gorman, granted under the Act of
Parliament called the Mulgoa Irrigation Act. This Act was passed by the Parliament of New South
Wales in December, 1890, and enables and authorises Messrs. George Chaffey and Henry Gorman
“ to establish a system of irrigation and water supply within and adjacent to the police district of
Penrith, to acquire lands to establish irrigation works, to authorise the sale and supply of water for
irrigation and domestic use, and to construct, make, and lay dams, weirs, or floodgates upon or across
the Nepean and Warragamba Rivers, and for all other purposes which may be incidental thereto.”
And 3. To exercise the rights under the- provisional contracts made by the promoters or on their behalf
with the owners of the lands proposed toi be irrigated, and which are set forth hereunder,
The estates which in the first instance-it is proposed to form into an Irrigation Colony (the first
in New South Wales), consisting of nchi,, fruit-growing, horticultural lands, suitable for the growth of
oranges, lemons, apricots, peaches, nectarines, vines, &c., in area about 20,000 acres in all, situated in
the County of Cumberland!, 30 miles or thereabouts from Sydney, in the parishes of Mulgoa, Clare-
mont, Melville, and. Rooty Hill,, and extending to-within a mile of the railway station of the pro-
sperous town of Penrith, and adjoining St. Mary’s on the Great Western Line of Railway, form the
key to the whole country round* Mulgoa. These can be irrigated from the Nepean and Warragamba
Rivers, andi i t is- proposed to. sell these lands in blocks of 5 and 10 acres and upwards, with water rights
attached. The-actual cost of the land will be under £l2 per acre, and the time required for carrying
out the works; so. a*s to. commence supplying water it is estimated will be within nine months. The
promoters confidently expect that these blocks,, with the water rights, will realise a profit of from £25
to, £5O per acre;.
The genuineness of this enterprise and the practical skill of the Messrs. Chaffey (the head of which
firm, Mr. George Chaffey, CIE., will be the consulting engineer) are sufficient guarantees that this
great creative work, the first in New South Wales, will be carried out in a profitable way, and on a
scalp-nob before attempted! in, this colony.,
Messrs. Chaffey Brothers have successfully established irrigation colonies at Etiwanda and
Onbarip,, in, California, where the lands improved by the growth of vineyards, orange and lemon
! groves, Sec,, have been sold at from £250 to £3OO per acre..
Messrs. Chaffey Brothers have also successfully established, and are- profitably developing on a
scale commensurate with the magnitude of the-enterprise, the irrigation colonies of' Mildura (in Vic-
toria, 500-miles,from Melbourne) and Renmark (in South Australia, 250 miles from Adelaide).
The Vendors,have agreed to transfer to, the Company the valuable- concessions, privileges, and
rights referred to, for the sum of £25,000, and 125,000 shares fully paid up-they have,, however; such
faith in the enterprise that they have-agreed'not to transfer 100,000 of the shares for a period of three
years from the incorporation, of. the Company.,

Facts Speak Louder than Words.
Read this sworn testimony from a well-known
citizen:—
Statutory Declaration.
“I, George Hewett, do hereby solemly and
sincerely declare as follows :
“That I feel it a duty I owe to the public to
state that I have been suffering for many years

out on the ebb-tide of alcohol, it was calculated
that he would not leave sufficient money to pay
his funeral expenses.

The irreverent Sydney correspondent of a
N.S.W. journal on Governor Jersey :
Jersey himself looks a good deal like the husband of a
blue stocking. He is a small, thin, weedy-looking man,
though he is the ninth Earl of his race. He has a rather
backward crop of golden hair—red some people call St-
and whiskers to match same. His eyes are weak, and his
nose suits them, and he is so mournfully thin that an
expert standing behind him could see right through him
if he had a pane in his chest. He is short, but has large
feet, and when walking turns his toes in so much that he
has to step very high for fear of tripping over himself.
He is said to have established a reputation as an athlete in
his youth, and is credited with having run a hundred
yards in very short metre. I scanned him carefully from
head to hock at the official landing, and I think there
must be some mistake, or else that when he put up the
record for pedestrianism someone must have been chasing
him with a wood-axe.
Recommended by doctors—Wolfe’s Schnapps.
A good story is told of Edward Jenks,
Dean of the Faculty of Law of Melbourne Uni-
versity. At the recent examinations Dr.
MTnerney was appointed co-examiner in Roman
law. Professor Jenks, after setting the paper in
that subject, convened a meeting of the board—
himself and MTnerney—to draw up a paper.
After taking the chair, Jenks advised his confrere
to move a resolution against any question in the
paper that he objected to. The latter did so in
regard to several questions, and a vote was taken
each time with this result: For the motion—Dr.
MTnerney ; against—Prof. Jenks in propria per-
sona. Prof. Jenks exercised his casting vote as
chairman. The Professor and the Dr. no longer
play marbles in the same alley.
If in need of a stimulant take Wolfe’s Schnapps,
One Douglas Freshfield, a pillar of the
Charity Organisation Society, in London, gives an
instance of the “abuses” to which General
Booth’s scheme will be liable. He points to the
report of a case recently brought before the
Coventry magistrates, wherein a police-superin-
tendent stated that “ the Warwickshire roads
swarmed with vagrants, all going to London with
the same idea of getting something from General
Booth.” To the cold, organising mind of Fresh-
field, whose business in life is the squelching of
applicants for charity, there is something very
reprehensible in a bare-footed tramp of 100 miles
through the snow, made by a man who cherishes
expectations of vetting something in the way of
food and work at the end of his journey.
Although not quite clear as to where the abuse
comes in (unless he means that these poor
wretches, padding the hoof from the Midland
counties will let off bad language if the General
has nothing for them) Freshfield is sure that any
scheme for administering relief upon presumptive
evidence of starvation, unsupported by certificates
from three parsons and four old ladies is neces-
sarily dangerous. The Charity Organisation
Society has been established for many years, dis-
pensing large sums of money for the purchase of
stamps and stationery, and supporting scores of
deserving cases in snug office billets, yet it has
never encouraged a lot of nasty vagrants to come
forward with the idea of obtaining assistance in
any shape or form. Booth’s manifesto has already
produced this shocking result, and creatures that
might otherwise have remained satisfied to die in
the ditches of their native heath are plodding
along towards “Lunnon,” snuffing an imaginative
odour of butcher’s meat in the frosty air. Like
the Piper of Hamelin, at whose shrill invitation
myriads of rats from their retreats came tumbling,
the “General” lures swarms of human rodents
into the open, to the dismay and disgust of smug
respectability. If the well-to-do classes were
anxious for a practical illustration of the length,
breadth and depth of England’s poverty then this
condition of the Warwickshire roads might be re-
garded as chief amongst the uses of the shadowy
“Salvation ” scheme. But they are riot. Where-
fore the police, magistrates, and other professional
philanthropists are shaking their heads and dis-
covering possible “ abuses ” of charity in the fact
that so many vagrants are travelling such a weary
distance with no better idea than to get something.
If they get sent empty back again, society can
have no fault to find with the scheme, and every-
thing will be quite right as before.

The well-known firm of James M‘Ewan
and Go., Limited, London and Melbourne, have
just opened a branch of their hardware and iron-
mongery business in Engine and Hay-streets,
Haymarket, Sydney, upder the. management of
Mr. A. Asher Smith. A large and stock
will shortly arrive. The advent of this old-
established house must prove a boon to the trade
in N.S.W.
The annual ocean race of the tea steamers
from Amoy to Australia with the first chops of
the season started on Jan. 30. Victoria is the
largest tea consumer in the world per head, with
111b; N.S. Wales, Queensland, and South Aus-
tralia following, with 91b., 81b., and 71b ; next
comes the Cape of Good Hope, with 51b., which is
about the average in China itself, where they put
away about 2000 million pounds annually, the
whole world’s consumption being 2500 million
pounds. England consumes about 21b. per head,
and the United States a good deal less, Russia
coming next to China and the Cape.
Dr. W. Maloney, who with all his little faults,
is an outspoken Radical, received a hearty send-
off on a pleasure trip to Europe from his West
Melbourne constituents. Premier Muuro shed
his cold smile upon the bun voyage ceremony, and
appeared to take the moral of Maloney’s popu-
larity to heart. West Melbourne wanted its re-
presentative, to be a warm member as well as a
native. The doctor is considered to have filled
the billet. He never worships the fatted calf at
Government House, and he was the first to cry
out against the military terrorism of strike time.
The late Duke of Somerset succeeded his
venerable brother, and his venerable brother suc-
ceeds him. This is becoming as monotonous as was
the succession of Louis XVI., Louis XVIII., and
Charles X. in France, or the horrid line of old
men, the Georges and William, broken up by the
girl, Queen Victoria, who used to be so nice. The
three old brothers, St. Maur, enjoy a dismal suc-
cession of dukerv. The first venerable died 1885,
after doing all the good he could with a pungent
little book demolishing Democracy, and another
smashing up religion, with the zeal of a Queens-
berry going for a cornsack— or a coryphee. This
Somerset was a combination of Squire Wendover
in “Robert Els mere,” with Lord Dorincourt in
“Little Lord Fauntleroy.” A whole swarm of
distant relations are getting ready with their
curled little darling Lord Fauntleroys.
About forty years ago —as the white-haired
parent of melodrama usually commences his awful
revelations —a Nepaulese ambassador arrived at
Southampton with presents, valued at a quarter
of a million sterling, for Victoria Regina. Lord
John Russell gave instructions that the black
gentleman’s personal effects and those of his suite
were to be passed at the Customs free of duty,
but subsequently countermanded the first order,
and said that only the “ royal presents” (like
Lady Loch’s jewels) were to pass without inspec-
tion. Thereupon the ambassador gave Old
England the alternative of foregoing duty on the
whole bag of tricks, or of seeing presents and all
taken back to Nepaul by the ship that br ught
them. Then the Queen went into hysterics and
Prince Albert shrieked “ Mein Gott!” and the
black gentleman received Royal Letters Patent,
or something, to bring the presents along on any
terms he darned well liked. This incident is not
recorded in any modern history.

Wagga Branch of the Amalgamated
Shearers’ Union received during last year £7,418
8s. 7d., of which amount £2,579 10s. represents
strike levies, cheerfully responded to. The
disbursements were £4,840 19s. Gd., the year end-
ing with a balance of £2 577 9s. Id.
Samuel Samper (the first Mayor of Cook-
town and of late years manager of the Austra-
lasian Mining Exchange, Sydney) died the other
days, aged 48 years, sincerely regretted by all who
knew him. He was a German by birth, and
arrived in Australia 30 years ago, when he became
a pioneer of Gulgonsr, and since then had “fol-
lowed the goldfields” in N.S.W., New Zealand,
and Queensland. Mr. Samper was a Jew,
possessed of all the kindness of heart which is so
often accounted a Christian monopoly, and if to
him in the Great Unknown is measured out the
mercy which, as a diggings storekeeper, he ever
meted to the miner who was down on his luck and
to the widow and children of the man who had
gone under, then will Sam. —to whose ashes peace !
—be happy for evermore.

Michael Davitb has been showing-up
O’Donovan Rossa, who was always in straits to
account for the disposal of the cash sent to him
for dynamiting purposes. Some 10 years ago
Queen Victoria, coming down stairs at Windsor
Castle, happened to slip and hurt her foot. The
news was cabled to New York, whereupon Rossa
interviewed himself, as usual, and declared that
one of his agents had been in the Castle the day
before and had soaped the stairs.

There is only one other ease oil record in
Australia where counsel instructed on one side
afterwards appeared for the other. Some years
ago Colonel Jeremiah Brazier, C.B., a retired
officer of the. Indian artillery—who had risen from
the ranks—was travelling in Australia with a
young friend, and while in Sydney they put up
for a few days at a hotel at Hyde. The Colonel
and his friend were about leaving for Maoriland,
and the latter failing to pay his hotel-bill a writ
was obtained and placed in the hands of a sheriff’s
officer who proceeded to the vessel to execute it.
The Colonel interfered to prevent the arrest of his
companion, and the officer unwisely arrested both
and lodged them in Darlinghurst. After 24 hours
detention the Colonel was released, and he imme-
diately entered an action against the sheriff. He
had Sir James Martin for him, while Attorney-
General Butler appeared for the Crown (or
sheriff), Sir Alfred Stephen was the judge. The
case had gone on for several days when a hitch
occurred and proceedings were stayed. Mean-
while, Sir Alfred resigned his position on the
bench, Parkes threw over Butler, to whom he had
promised the appointment, and gave it to Martin.
Butler, of course, resigned office, and Innes suc-
ceeded him. When the Brazier case came on
again Innes appeared for the Crown and Butler
for the Colonel. The latter won and got damages,
but the Government fought inch by inch, failing
finally before the Privy Council. The imputa-
tion by the Crown was that Brazier and his com-
panion were travelling and living immorally.
A deadbeat lately called at a shanty out
West looking for work, and was pi’omptly told
there wasn’t any. “Well,” said the sundowner,
“have you got any rats about this place?”
“ Rats ! Did you say rats ?” asked the shanty-
owner—•“ Rats ? why the place is alive with ’em.”
“ Well,’’exclaimed the deadbeat, “I’m your man.”
“Right you are; then start away and clear’em
out,” said the publican. “Good,” rejoined the
deadbeat. All day long he pottered about im-
bibing gratuitous beers and getting outside tooth-
some grub, yet made he no move towards shifting
the rats. Next morning the publican interviewed
him. “Touching the rats, I thought you were
going to make ’em scarce?” “Ah, yes,” said
the deadbeat, “about the rats; I reckon as
soon as I begin a rat will be a curiosity
about here.” “Well, begin as soon as you
like,” murmured the publican. “Right,” replied
the deadbeat. Another day passed during which
the deadbeat absorbed more eleemosynary grub
and beer, yet he made no attempt on the rats.
The second morning came and the publican again
interviewed him. “About those rats,” he re-
marked. “Ah, yes,” said the loafer, “touching
these rats. Well, as I said afore, this place won’t
be much troubled in the future by rats as soon as
I make a start on ’em.” “ That be d d !” said
the publican, “your game is pretty well played
out. Are you going to begin, or are you not ? ”
“Am I going to begin,” asked the deadbeat.
“Am I going to begin—just you get me what I
want and I’ll make those rats d d scarce.”
“What do you want ? ” said the publican, “Eetch
me a piece of hoop-iron,” said the deadbeat, “ so
long ’’—measuring the length with his arm. The
publican brought the hoop-iron to him. The loafer
weighed it, straightened it, felt the edge and
swished it about. “ That’ll do,” he said. “ Wow
bring on your rats ! ”
